The Jabariyya Objection Backfires
The author responds to the second objection by showing it backfires.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the Jabariyya's objection by demonstrating that their own position leads to the denial of all causal influence.

Core Idea
A charge that backfires is worse than the original accusation.

In this passage, the author addresses the second objection from the Jabariyya. Rather than defending against it, he demonstrates that if their charge were true, it would imply something far more unacceptable from their own perspective. This is a classic argumentative move: showing that the opponent's position leads to a self-defeating conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'
2
Identify the structure of the argument
The author says: 'If it follows from your doctrine X, then from your doctrine follows Y.' Find X and Y.
X is 'that the Maker is an agent by essence', Y is 'denial of all effect and allowance of preponderance without a preponderant'.
3
Understand why Y is worse
The author claims Y is 'uglier and more decisive' than the original charge. Ask yourself why denying all causation is more absurd than saying God acts necessarily.
Denying causation undermines all rational explanation and divine wisdom.
4
Reflect on the rhetorical move
The author does not defend against the charge; he shows that the charge, if accepted, leads to a more damaging consequence for the accuser. This is a turn-about argument.
This type of argument is common in theological debates to show inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أن الجواب المعتمد عن الوجه الثاني : إنا بينا فيما تقدم : أنه إن لزم على مذهب الجبرية القول بأن الصانع موجب بالذات، لا فاعل بالاختيار، لزمهم على مذهبهم : نفي المؤثر بالكلية، وتجويز أن يترجح أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا لمرجح . ولا شك أن هذا أقبح وأقطع مما ألزموه علينا .
And know that the relied-upon answer regarding the second aspect: We have explained previously that if it follows from the doctrine of the Jabariyya to say that the Maker is an agent by essence, not a free agent, then it follows from their doctrine: the denial of the effect entirely, and the allowance that one of the two sides of the possible might preponderate over the other without a preponderant. And there is no doubt that this is uglier and more decisive than what they have imposed upon us.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is the author's response to the second objection. He says that even if the jabariyya charge that their view makes God a necessary agent is accepted, then on their own doctrine it would follow that they must deny any efficient cause altogether and allow that one of the two possibilities (e.g., existence or nonexistence) can prevail without any preponderating factor. This is worse and more decisive in refuting their position than the charge they leveled against us.

Key Terms
موجب بالذات
فاعل بالاختيار
نفي المؤثر
ترجيح بلا مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author is conceding that the Ash'ari view makes God a necessary agent.
Correction: The author does not concede; instead he shows that even if it did, the Jabariyya's own position leads to a more absurd consequence.

Checkpoint
What does the author say follows from the jabariyya doctrine regarding causation?
A) That God is a free agent.
B) That effects can occur without any cause.
C) That human actions are determined.
D) That the world is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the Jabariyya's objection by demonstrating that their own position entails a denial of all causal influence, which is worse than the alleged problem.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that your actions are meaningless because everything is already decreed? How does the author's warning against denying all causal influence challenge that feeling, and what specific step will you take this week to act as though your choices matter before Allah?
?
When you face a situation where the causes seem hidden, do you tend to demand a visible reason or do you trust Allah's wisdom? This lesson shows that denying causality leads to absurdity. Where in your life do you need to strengthen your trust in the means Allah has placed without falling into either extreme of fatalism or independent causation?
?
The Jabariyya's objection backfires because it forces them to accept a more absurd consequence. Think of a time when you criticized someone but later realized the same criticism applied more strongly to yourself. How does this passage encourage you to examine your own beliefs before accusing others of error?

The Boomerang Refutation: Jabariyya Motive Objection Turned Against Them
Author refutes third objection by showing self-contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author turns the Jabariyya objection about motive against them by showing it leads to the same problem of arbitrary specification.

Core Idea
The Jabariyya's denial of motive dependence boomerangs back on them.

In the ongoing debate about causality and divine action, the author now addresses the third objection from his opponents. He turns their own argument against them by showing that their position leads to an identical difficulty. Let's examine his concise rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: 'As for the third aspect... its answer is that...'
The passage is a single sentence with a logical conditional.
2
Identify the Jabariyya claim
The Jabariyya view is that the occurrence of an act does not depend on a motive (al-daa'i). Highlight this key phrase.
3
Understand the consequence
The author says that then the same problem (arbitrary specification) follows for them. Ask: why would that be? Because without a motive, nothing explains why the act occurs at one time rather than another.
This shows the objection applies to both sides.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الوجه الثالث من الوجوه التي نسبناها إليهم : فجوابه : إن مذهبهم : أن حصول الفعل لا يتوقف على الداعي وحينئذ يلزمهم على هذا المذهب، عين ما ألزموه علينا .
As for the third aspect among the aspects we attributed to them: its answer is that their doctrine is that the occurrence of an act does not depend on the motive. And then it follows from this doctrine exactly what they imposed upon us.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is the response to the third objection. The jabariyya say that an act's occurrence does not depend on a motive (al-daa'i). If that is the case, then the same problem of arbitrary specification applies—they cannot explain why an act occurs at one time rather than another. Thus, the charge they made against others (arbitrary specification without cause) applies equally to them.

Key Terms
الداعي
توقف الفعل
إلزام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author is conceding the objection and admitting a problem in his own view.
Correction: The author does not admit the problem; he shows that the same problem applies to the Jabariyya. The argument is a tu quoque (you too) refutation.

Checkpoint
What is the author's response to the third aspect?
A) He admits the problem.
B) He argues that the jabariyya view on motive leads to the same difficulty.
C) He denies that actions have motives.
D) He says the issue is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the third objection by showing that the Jabariyya denial of motive dependence leads to the very problem they accuse others of.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever hold others to a standard of consistency that you do not apply to yourself? In what area of your beliefs or actions are you most likely to demand logical coherence from someone else while excusing your own contradictions? What would it take for you to remove that double standard starting today?
?
The Jabariyya failed to see that their own claim led to the very problem they criticized. Is there a belief you hold that you have not fully thought through? Are you afraid to examine its consequences because it might force you to change something comfortable in your life? Will you commit to honestly tracing the implications of one of your core beliefs this week?
?
This lesson is a reminder that objections often boomerang. When you next feel the urge to criticize someone's position, pause and ask: 'Is my own position vulnerable to the same criticism?' Can you think of a recent criticism you made that might actually apply to you? What would it mean to humbly acknowledge that?

Counter-Objection: The Objection Also Rebuts the Mu'tazila
Author counters Mu'tazila by showing their own inconsistencies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection against the Ash'arite view of causality is turned back against the Mu'tazila on two specific points.

Core Idea
A counter-argument that turns the tables: the same objection used against Ash'arites applies even more forcefully to the Mu'tazila.

This passage presents a clever rebuttal. The Mu'tazila object that the Ash'arite denial of human creative power undermines prophethood. The author shows that the Mu'tazila themselves face greater difficulties: if humans can create, miracles lose their divine signature; if actions require motivation, determinism follows. Let's examine these two points.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
This passage is a counter-objection to the Mu'tazila who argued that the Ash'arite view (that God creates all actions) makes it impossible to know that miracles come from God. The author now turns this argument back on them.
Remember that the Mu'tazila believe humans have the power to create their own actions.
2
Analyze the first point
The author argues: if the Mu'tazila admit that human capacity can create (i.e., produce effects), then they cannot be certain that miracles are from God. Because if a human can create, perhaps the miracle was created by a human. The author says they already refuted the Mu'tazila proof that only God creates bodies and life.
In contrast, the Ash'arite position that humans do not create removes this doubt.
3
Analyze the second point
The second point concerns motivation (داعي). If the Mu'tazila hold that action depends on motivation, then when there is no motivation, no action occurs. This implies that the agent is compelled to act only when motivation exists, which is a form of determinism (جبر).
The text cuts off mid-explanation, but the gist is clear: the Mu'tazila would be forced into determinism.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Notice how the author turns the objection around. Instead of defending the Ash'arite view directly, he shows that the Mu'tazila's own principles lead to worse contradictions. This is a classic dialectical move in kalām.
The argument reinforces the Ash'arite position that only God is the true creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أن المعتمد في الجواب عن هذا المقام ، أن نقول : هذا الذي الزمتموه علينا ، لازم عليكم من وجوه : الأول : إنكم لما اعترفتم بأن قدرة العبد صالحة للإيجاد . لم يمكنكم القطع بأن فاعل هذه المعجزات هو الله تعالى . ونحن قد حكينا في باب دلائلنا العقلية : دليل المعتزلة على أن غير الله تعالى، لا يصح منه خلق الجسم والحياة . وبينا ضعفه وسقوطه . وأما نحن [ فلما ] قلنا : إن قدرة العبد لا تصلح للإيجاد ، لم يتوجه هذا السؤال علينا البتة . فثبت : أن دلالة المعجز على صدق المدعي ، إنما تتم على مذهبنا ، لا على مذهبكم . الثاني : إن المعتزلة . إما أن يحكموا بأن الفعل يتوقف على الداعي ، أو لا يحكموا بذلك. فإن كان الأول لزمهم الجبر . وذلك لأن عند عدم الداعي
Know that the reliable answer regarding this station is that we say: This which you have necessitated upon us is necessary upon you from aspects: The first: Since you have acknowledged that the servant's capacity is valid for creation, you cannot be certain that the agent of these miracles is God, exalted. And we have recounted in the chapter of our rational proofs: the proof of the Mu'tazila that it is not valid for other than God to create a body and life. And we have shown its weakness and collapse. As for us, since we have said that the servant's capacity is not suitable for creation, this question does not arise against us at all. Thus it is established that the indication of the miracle to the truth of the claimant is only completed according to our doctrine, not according to your doctrine. The second: The Mu'tazila either rule that the action depends on motivation, or they do not. If the first, then determinism (jabr) is necessary for them. That is because when there is no motivation...
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage presents the author's response to the objection that the Ash'arite view negates prophethood. The author states that the objection also applies to the Mu'tazila from two angles. First, if the Mu'tazila acknowledge that the servant's capacity can create (i.e., produce effects), then they cannot be certain that miracles are from God, because the servant could potentially create them. The author mentions that they have already shown the weakness of the Mu'tazila proof that only God can create bodies and life. In contrast, since Ash'arites hold that the servant's capacity does not create (i.e., God creates all actions), this problem does not arise. Therefore, miracles indicate truth only on the Ash'arite view. Second, regarding motivation: if the Mu'tazila hold that actions depend on motivation, they fall into determinism (jabr) because when motivation is absent, the action does not occur, making the servant compelled.

Key Terms
قدرة العبد
إيجاد
معجزات
دلالة المعجز
داعي
جبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the author is admitting the Ash'arite position is weak and trying to deflect.
Correction: This is not an admission; it is a counter-argument showing the opponent's inconsistency. The author demonstrates that the Mu'tazila's own views lead to greater problems.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does the Mu'tazila view lead to doubt about miracles?
A) Because they deny God's power.
B) Because if human capacity can create, one cannot be sure miracles are from God.
C) Because they reject miracles.
D) Because they believe in determinism.

Key Takeaway
The objection that the Ash'arite view negates prophethood is turned back on the Mu'tazila: their own views on human capacity and motivation lead to greater problems.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you rely on your own ability to achieve something, do you ever stop to consider that your power is limited and that all true creation belongs to God? How does that affect your trust in divine guidance over your own plans?
?
The author shows that believing humans can create their actions leads to uncertainty about God's miracles. In your life, where might you be claiming a power that is only God's, and what would it mean to acknowledge your complete dependence on Him?
?
If the Mu'tazila's theory of motivation leads logically to determinism, then what assumptions in your own thinking might, if traced to their conclusion, contradict what you believe about God and free will? Are you willing to examine those assumptions honestly?

The Objection from Estimation and Its Rebuttal
Razi responds to the objection that creation means estimation with two rebuttals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the claim that God's creation is merely estimation and not origination of human actions.

Core Idea
Creation entails origination, not mere estimation.

In this passage, Razi addresses a subtle objection: perhaps 'creation' means 'estimation,' not 'bringing into existence.' He provides two powerful rebuttals to show that estimation itself requires origination. Let's examine his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation to get a feel for the argument's flow.
Pay attention to the objection and the two rebuttals.
2
Identify the objection
Find where the opponent says creation is only estimation. Note their stance.
3
Analyze the first rebuttal
See how Razi argues that estimation implies thought/conjecture, impossible for God, so creation must mean origination.
Follow the logic step by step.
4
Analyze the second rebuttal and answers
Even if creation means estimation, estimation requires an originator. Then see Razi's clarifying answers to both objections.
The answers refine the meaning of estimation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : دليلكم ينتج أن فعل العبد مخلوق الله تعالى . ونحن نقول بموجبه . وذلك لأن الخلق عبارة عن التقدير. وعندنا : أن أفعال العباد واقعة بتقدير الله تعالى ، وإن كانت حاصلة بإيجاد العبد وإحداثه . لا يقال : هذا مدفوع من وجهين : الأول : إن التقدير عبارة عن الفكرة والروية والظن والحسبان . وذلك في حق الله تعالى ممتنع . ولما امتنع حمل كونه خالقـاً لافعال العباد ، على معنى التقدير ، وجب حمله على معنى الإحداث الثاني : هب أن كونه تعالى خالقاً لأفعال العباد ، عبارة عن كونه تعالى مقدراً لها إلا أن التقدير عبارة عن إيقاع ذلك الفعل في زمان مخصوص ، ومحل مخصوص ، على وجه مخصوص . وهذا المعنى لا يتأتى إلا من كان محدثاً للفعل . فلما ثبت بهذا النص : أنه تعالى مقدر الأفعال العباد ، وثبت بالعقل : أن مقدر الأفعال يجب أن يكون موجداً لها ، لزم من مجموع الأمرين كونه تعالى موجداً لأفعال العباد . لأنا نقول : أما الأول . فجوابه : إن التقدير عبارة عن إيقاع الفعل على قدر مخصوص . وهذا المعنى لا يحصل إلا بالعلم أو الظن . ثم إن كان العلم حاصلاً ، كما في حق الله تعالى، فلا حاجة إلى الفكرة ، وإن لم يكن حاصلاً كما في حق العبد . فهناك يحتاج إلى الفكرة لتحصيل ذلك العلم ، أو ذلك الظن وأما السؤال الثاني : فجوابه : أن نقول : إنه تعالى مقدر لأفعال العباد . أنه تعالى حاكم بوقوعها على تلك المقادير ، كما يقال : فلان يكفر فلاناً ، بمعنى أو يضلله . إذا حكم بكفره وضلاله
If it is said: Your proof yields that the action of the servant is created by God Almighty, and we affirm it. That is because creation is estimation. According to us, the actions of servants occur by God's estimation, even if they are produced by the servant's existence and origination. It is not said: This is rebutted from two aspects: First: Estimation is thought, deliberation, opinion, and conjecture. That is impossible with respect to God Almighty. Since applying His being Creator of the actions of servants to the meaning of estimation is impossible, it must be applied to the meaning of origination. Second: Suppose that His being Creator of the actions of servants means He is their Estimator, yet estimation is the placing of that action at a specific time, a specific place, and in a specific manner. This meaning is only achieved by one who is the originator of the action. Since it is established by this text that He is the Estimator of the actions of servants, and established by reason that the Estimator of actions must be their Exister, the combination of the two entails that He is the Exister of the actions of servants. For we say: As for the first, its answer: Estimation is the placing of the action upon a specific measure. This meaning is only achieved by knowledge or opinion. Then if knowledge is present, as in the case of God Almighty, there is no need for thought; and if it is not present, as in the case of the servant, there is need for thought to acquire that knowledge or opinion. As for the second question, its answer: We say that He is the Estimator of the actions of servants means that He is the Judge of their occurrence upon those measures, just as it is said: So-and-so declares so-and-so a disbeliever, or leads him astray, meaning he judges him to be a disbeliever and astray.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage handles an objection and the author's response. The opponent concedes that the servant's action is created by God, but only in the sense of estimation (taqdeer), not actual origination. The author then presents two rebuttals. The first rebuttal argues that estimation in human terms involves thought and conjecture, which are impossible for God; thus, 'creation' in the verse must mean origination. The second rebuttal argues that even if 'creation' means estimation, estimation requires specific time, place, and manner, which can only be achieved by the one who originates the act. Therefore, the text and reason together prove that God is the originator. The author then gives detailed answers: for the first, he clarifies that estimation simply means placing on a measure, and God's knowledge suffices without thought. For the second, he explains that 'estimation' here means God's judgment that the act will occur in a certain way, not the act of origination itself.

Key Terms
تقدير
إحداث
فكرة
ظن
حاكم
مقدر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Razi's rebuttal contradicts his own view by accepting estimation as creation.
Correction: Razi clarifies that estimation implies origination or is merely judgment, aligning with his position.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does 'estimation' (taqdeer) ultimately require?
A) Thought and deliberation
B) The presence of knowledge or opinion
C) The act of the servant alone
D) A specific time only

Key Takeaway
God's estimation of actions necessitates His origination of them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you plan your day, do you subconsciously feel that you are the one determining the details—when and where things happen—or do you truly believe that God is the only one who can fix the exact measure of your actions? What would change in your heart if you fully accepted that your estimation of your own life is merely borrowed from His?
?
Razi argues that estimation requires knowledge without thought for God. Do you live as if God knows every minute detail of your actions without needing to deliberate, or do you act as if He is surprised by your choices? How does this truth affect your sense of being constantly seen and held accountable?
?
If even your own ‘creation’ of an action is just an acquisition, not an origination, then where do you place your pride when you succeed? Can you, when you accomplish something good, genuinely attribute it to God’s origination while still feeling responsible? What would it look like to live with that tension today?

Linguistic Ambiguity of 'ما' and Its Theological Resolution
Conclusion of a linguistic argument for the object meaning of 'ما'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the linguistic ambiguity of the word 'ما' (source vs. object) is resolved by contextual analysis to support a theological argument against human autonomy in actions.

Core Idea
Context determines which meaning of 'ما' is correct for theological clarity.

In this passage, the author presents a linguistic argument to settle a theological dispute. The word 'ما' can mean either the action itself (source) or the object of action. By examining the verse's context, the author shows that interpreting 'ما' as the object is more appropriate, thereby undermining the claim that humans create their own actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the author refers to three proofs that were previously established; focus on the conclusion and reasoning here.
2
Identify the two meanings of 'ما'
The passage states that 'ما' can come as either a source (masdar) or an object (maf'ul). Understand how each interpretation affects the theological claim.
The source interpretation implies the verse is about the act of worship itself; the object interpretation focuses on what is worshipped.
3
Understand the contextual preference
The author argues that interpreting 'ما' as the object is better because the surrounding verses discuss idol worship, not the creation of deeds. See how context resolves the ambiguity.
This shows that the verse does not prove humans create their own acts; it only condemns false objects of worship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فثبت بهذه الوجوه الثلاثة : أن ( ما ) مع ما بعده ، كما يجيء بمعنى المصدر فقد يجيء بمعنى المفعول . فلم يكن حمله ههنا على المصدر أولى من حمله على المفعول . بل نقول : حمله ههنا على المفعول أولى . وذلك لأن المقصود في هذه الآية تزييف مذهبهم في عبادة الأصنام، لا بيان أنهم لا يوجدون أفعال أنفسهم . لأن الذي جرى ذكره في أول الآية إلى هذا الموضع هو مسألة عبادة الأصنام ، لا مسألة خلق الأعمال
It is established by these three ways: that 'ma' together with what follows, just as it comes in the meaning of the source (masdar), so it comes in the meaning of the object (maf'ul). So it was not more appropriate to interpret it here as the source than to interpret it as the object. Rather, we say: interpreting it here as the object is more appropriate. That is because the intended purpose in this verse is to invalidate their doctrine in worshipping idols, not to show that they do not bring about their own actions. For what has been mentioned from the beginning of the verse to this point is the matter of worshipping idols, not the matter of the creation of deeds.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage presents a linguistic argument to resolve a theological dispute about human actions. The author asserts that three proofs (the context of the phrase has previously mentioned three points, but they are not explicitly repeated here; they refer to earlier arguments on language usage) demonstrate that the Arabic word 'ما' can have two meanings: it can be an infinitive (masdar) meaning 'your doing' or an object (maf'ul) meaning 'what you do'. The opponents had insisted it must be infinitival, implying that humans are the agents of their worship. The author counters that both interpretations are possible, and in this particular verse, interpreting it as the object is more appropriate because the verse's context is about renouncing idol worship, not about human autonomy in creating actions. Thus the verse does not prove that humans create their own acts; it only condemns the objects of worship.

Key Terms
ما
المصدر
المفعول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The linguistic ambiguity of 'ما' is irrelevant to the theological meaning of the verse.
Correction: On the contrary, the ambiguity determines whether the verse is about the act of worship or the objects worshipped. The author shows that context resolves it, and that the object interpretation undermines the claim of human autonomy.

Checkpoint
What are the two possible meanings of 'ما' in this argument?
A) مفعول and مصدر
B) اسم and فعل
C) مفرد and جمع
D) عام and خاص

Key Takeaway
The word 'ما' can be interpreted as either the source or the object, and context determines which is intended, affecting the theological understanding of the verse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson reveals how a single word can shift the entire meaning of a verse. Are you willing to pause and examine the Arabic and context of the Qur'anic passages you often rely on, even if it challenges your current understanding?
?
The author uses the verse's central purpose to choose the correct interpretation. In your own life, when you read the Qur'an, do you let the overarching purpose of the text shape your understanding, or do you impose your own biases? What one step can you take this week to study a verse more deeply before forming an opinion?
?
The verse is about invalidating idol worship, not about human actions. Are there areas in your beliefs where you have inadvertently focused on secondary issues while missing the primary message? How can you realign your focus to what Allah prioritizes in His Book?

The Twelfth Proof and the Objection about Containment
The author presents the proof then the objection about the word 'في'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the twelfth proof and the opponent's linguistic objection based on the word 'في'.

Core Idea
The verse 'He created for you all that is in the earth' may include actions, but 'في' requires containment.

This lesson presents a key proof used by proponents of the createdness of human actions and the immediate objection from the opposition. The debate centers on the interpretation of the preposition 'fi' (in). We will see how the proof attempts to include actions by indirect containment and how the opponent restricts 'fi' to physical containment in the earth.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the literal translation. Let the argument unfold naturally.
Notice the flow from proof to objection to concession.
2
Identify the proof
The proof argues: actions exist in selves, selves in earth, therefore actions are in the earth, so the verse includes them.
This is a syllogistic chain.
3
Understand the objection
The opponent says 'في' means containment like a container, so only physical objects deposited in the earth are included, not accidental actions.
They cite linguistic consensus.
4
Notice the concession and homonymy argument
The proponent concedes 'في' can cover both meanings but argues it becomes homonymous, so it cannot be used for both without evidence.
This shifts the burden of proof.
5
Consider the challenge
The proponent asks why one interpretation is preferred. This sets up the following three arguments.
The lesson ends with this challenge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية عشر : قوله تعالى : ( خلق لكم ما في الأرض جميعاً ) وأفعال العباد موجودة في ذواتهم ، وذواتهم موجودة في الأرض . والموجود في الموجود في الشيء ، موجود في الشيء . فصح أن أفعال العباد موجودة في الأرض . فقوله تعالى : ( خلق لكم ما في الأرض جميعاً ) لم يدل على كونه تعالى خالقاً لأعمال العباد . فإن قيل : أهل اللغة نصوا على أن كلمة ( في ) للظرفية . وإنما يدخل في هذه الآية : ما يكون حاصلاً في الأرض ، حصول المظروف في الظرف . وهي الأشياء المودعة في الأرض. كالحيوانات والجمادات . أما أفعال العباد ، فهي أعراض ، ولا حصول لها في الأرض، على وفق حصول المظروف في الظرف بل على وفق حصول العرض في المحل . سلمنا : أن كلمة ( في ) تتناول ما يحصل في الشيء ، حصول الدرّة في الحقة . وما يحصل في الشيء حصول العرض في المحل . إلا أن هذين المعنيين لفظاً معنيان مختلفان بالماهية ، فكان لفظ ( في ) بالنسبة إلى هذين المفهومين ، مشتركاً . وحمل اللفظ المشترك على كل مفهوميه غير جائز . فلم كان حمله على ما ذكرتم أولى من حمله على ما ذكرنا ؟ وعليكم الترجيح.. بل ما ذكرناه أولى ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إن اللام في قوله ( لكم ) لعود المنفعة . وهذا لا يطرد في الكفر والفسوق والعصيان ، لأنها غير نافعة للعباد ، بل ضارة لهم . الثاني : إن قوله : ( خلق لكم ) يقتضي أن تلك الأشياء صارت مخلوقة ، وحصل الفراغ منها ، وأفعال العباد ليست كذلك . الثالث : إن قوله : ( خلق لكم ما في الأرض ) يقتضي كون كل واحد من هذه المخلوقات مخلوقاً لكل أحد. وأفعال العباد ليست كذلك . لأنه لا انتفاء بكل واحد منها إلا لفاعله فقط . والله أعلم . والجواب : إن كلمة ( في ) مستعملة في قولنا : الماء في الكوز ، وفي قولنا : العرض
The twelfth proof: His saying, exalted: 'He created for you all that is in the earth.' And the actions of servants exist in their selves, and their selves exist in the earth. And what exists in something existing in a thing exists in that thing. So it is correct that the actions of servants exist in the earth. So His saying: 'He created for you all that is in the earth' does not indicate that He, exalted, is the creator of the actions of servants. For if it is said: The people of language have stipulated that the word 'fi' is for containment. And only what occurs in the earth in the manner of a container and its contained enters under this verse. These are things deposited in the earth, like animals and inanimate objects. As for the actions of servants, they are accidents, and they do not have presence in the earth in accordance with the presence of a contained in a container, but rather in accordance with the presence of an accident in a substrate. We concede that the word 'fi' covers what occurs in a thing like the occurrence of a pearl in a box, and what occurs in a thing like the occurrence of an accident in a substrate. However, these two meanings are lexically two meanings different in essence, so the word 'fi' with respect to these two concepts is homonymous. And interpreting a homonymous word in both of its meanings is not permissible. Why then was its interpretation according to what you mentioned more appropriate than its interpretation according to what we mentioned? And the burden of proof is on you. Rather, what we mentioned is more appropriate, and it is indicated by three aspects: First: The lam in His saying 'for you' is for the return of benefit. This does not apply consistently to disbelief, sin, and disobedience, because they are not beneficial to the servants, but rather harmful to them. Second: His saying 'He created for you' implies that those things became created and were finished, and the actions of servants are not like that. Third: His saying 'He created for you all that is in the earth' implies that each one of these creatures is created for everyone. And the actions of servants are not like that, because no one benefits from each of them except their doer alone. And God knows best. And the answer: Indeed, the word 'fi' is used in our saying 'water in the jug' and in our saying 'the accident in the substrate'...
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage presents the twelfth proof and an objection-response. First, the proof: The verse 'He created for you all that is in the earth' is claimed to include human actions because human selves are in the earth, and their actions are in their selves, thus indirectly in the earth. The opponent objects that the preposition 'fi' (in) denotes containment like a container, but human actions are accidents that do not reside in the earth the way physical objects do—they reside in their subjects (the human body). The proponent then concedes that 'fi' can cover both meanings (placement in a container and inherence in a substrate) but argues that the word 'fi' would then be homonymous between two distinct concepts. It is not permissible to interpret a homonymous word in both senses simultaneously without evidence. The opponent's claim that it means only containment is not justified; the proponent's interpretation is superior, supported by three points: (1) the 'lam' in 'lakum' indicates benefit, but sinful actions are not beneficial; (2) 'created for you' implies completion and readiness, while human actions are ongoing; (3) 'the earth' implies general benefit for all, but each person's actions benefit only themselves. Therefore, the verse does not intend actions. The answer begins by asserting that 'fi' is used for both types of location, supporting the possibility of including actions. The response is incomplete here, but the main argument is presented.

Key Terms
في
الظرفية
العرض
المحل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The opponent's objection is that actions are not in the earth at all.
Correction: The objection is not that actions are not in the earth at all, but that they are not in the earth in the sense of containment like physical objects. They exist in the earth via inherence in their subjects.

Checkpoint
What is the opponent's objection regarding the word 'في' in the verse?
A) It is ambiguous
B) It only means containment
C) It cannot be applied to actions
D) It is metaphorical

Key Takeaway
The word 'في' is central to this debate; the opponent restricts it to physical containment, while the proponent argues it can include inherence, leading to a homonymy problem.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'He created for you all that is in the earth', do you automatically assume it refers only to physical, tangible things? Why do you instinctively exclude your own actions from that 'all'? What does that say about your sense of God's sovereignty over your choices?
?
The opponent here uses a technical linguistic point to avoid a conclusion they find uncomfortable. In what areas of your belief—perhaps about free will or divine decree—do you rely on convenient interpretations to avoid confronting a harder truth? Are you willing to demand proof of yourself as the proponent demands here?
?
You have just read a dense theological argument about a single preposition. How often do you pause to examine the language of the Quran with this level of precision? What one verse this week will you commit to studying with similar attention, not to win an argument but to draw closer to the One who sent it?

Three Arguments Why the Verse Does Not Include Actions
The author gives three arguments for why the verse excludes actions, then begins the answer about the word 'في'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three linguistic arguments that support the proponent's claim that the verse does not refer to human actions.

Core Idea
The verse's wording implies benefit, completion, and universal benefit, none of which apply to sinful actions.

After challenging the opponent, the proponent offers three distinct reasons why the verse 'He created for you all that is in the earth' should not be interpreted as including human actions. These arguments focus on the implications of the words 'for you' and 'created'. The lesson also begins the author's response concerning the word 'في'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic for the three arguments and the beginning of the answer. Notice the enumeration.
The three arguments are marked by 'الأول', 'الثاني', 'الثالث'.
2
Understand the first argument (benefit)
The lam in 'lakum' indicates benefit, but sinful actions are harmful, so they cannot be the intended object of creation.
Think about the theological implications.
3
Understand the second argument (completion)
The verb 'created' implies the act is finished and complete, but human actions are ongoing and contingent.
Consider the difference between creation and occurrence.
4
Understand the third argument (universal benefit)
The phrase 'all that is in the earth' suggests general benefit for everyone, whereas each action benefits only its doer.
This highlights the universal scope of the verse.
5
Examine the beginning of the answer
The author begins to respond that 'في' is used for both containment and inherence, supporting the possibility of including actions.
This part is incomplete, but it sets up the next stage.

Refuting the Objection of Seeking Divine Favors
The author refutes the objection that the verse could be about seeking divine favors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three counterarguments against interpreting divine making (ja'l) as seeking divine favors (al-alṭāf), and see how the final argument supports the author's deterministic position.

Core Idea
Seeking already given favors is absurd; true favors necessitate action.

This passage directly refutes an opponent's claim that a verse could be about seeking divine favors. The author provides three crisp counterarguments, the third being a sophisticated logical analysis. As we unpack them, watch how the author forces the opponent into a dilemma that ends up affirming his own determinism.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and first two arguments
Start by reading the full passage in Arabic and translation. Notice the first argument is linguistic (the word 'ja'l' is tied to Islam), the second is based on the opponent's own principle (God already does all favors).
2
Unpack the third argument step by step
The third argument is a conditional chain: if favors have effect, then obligation; if not, they are irrelevant. Use the guided breakdown and flow diagram to follow each step.
Pay attention to the sub-argument about preponderance without preponderator.
3
Connect to the author's doctrine
The passage ends by affirming that action is necessary when ability and motive combine. See how the whole argument circles back to this deterministic conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: ولما لا يجوز حمله على طلب الألطاف؟ قلنا: هذا أيضا مدفوع. الأول: إن لفظ الجعل مضاف إلى الإسلام، فصرفه إلى غيره مخالفة. الثاني: إن عندكم كل ما أمكن فعله من الألطاف، فقد فعله الله فحمل هذا السؤال على طلب الألطاف يكون طلبا لتحصيل الحاصل. وهو محال. الثالث: إن تلك الألطاف إما أن يكون لها أثر في ترجيح جانب الفعل على جانب الترك، أو لا يكون. فإن لم يكن لها أثر في هذا الترجيح، كان أمرا أجنبيا عن الإيمان، جاريا مجرى نعيق الغراب، وصرير الباب. وأما إن كان لتلك الألطاف أثر في الترجيح، فنقول: متى حصل الرجحان، فقد حصل الوجوب. وذلك لأن مع حصول ذلك القدر من الترجيح إما أن يجب الفعل أو يمتنع، أو لا يجب ولا يمتنع. فإن وجب فهو المطلوب. وإن امتنع فهو مانع مرجح. وإن لم يجب ولم يمتنع، فحينئذ يمكن وقوع الفعل معه تارة، ووقوعه أخرى. فلا اختصاص أحد الوقتين بالوقوع والوقت الآخر باللا وقوع إن كان لانضمام أمر آخر إليه كان اللطف المؤثر في الترجيح هو المجموع الحاصل مما حصل. ومن هذه الضمانة [؟] فلا يكون الذي فرضناه حاصلا، مؤثرا البتة في شيء من الترجيح، وكنا فرضناه كذلك. هذا خلف. وإن لم يكن لانضمام قيد آخر إليه، لزم الرجحان من غير مرجح. وهو محال. فثبت: أن عند حصول ذلك اللطف يجب حصول الفعل، وعند عدمه يمتنع. وذلك يعود إلى ما ذكرناه من أن حصول الفعل عند مجموع القدرة والداعي واجب. وذلك هو عين مذهبنا
His statement: Why is it not permissible to carry it on seeking divine favors (al-alṭāf)? We say: This too is refuted. First: The word 'ja'l' is attached to Islam, so turning it to other than it is a violation. Second: According to you, every favor that is possible to do, God has done it. So carrying this question on seeking favors would be a request to obtain what is already obtained, and that is impossible. Third: These favors either have an effect in tipping the scale towards action rather than omission, or they do not. If they have no effect in this tipping, then it is something extraneous to faith, like the croaking of a raven or the creaking of a door. But if those favors have an effect in tipping, we say: whenever preponderance occurs, obligation occurs. That is because with the occurrence of that amount of tipping, either the action becomes obligatory, or it becomes impossible, or it is neither obligatory nor impossible. If it becomes obligatory, that is what is sought. If it becomes impossible, then there is a preventing preponderator. If it is neither obligatory nor impossible, then at that time the action can sometimes occur and sometimes not. So the specification of one time for occurrence and the other for non-occurrence, if it is due to the addition of some other matter, then the favor effective in tipping is the total obtained from what has been obtained. And from this guarantee [؟], that which we assumed to be obtained would not be effective at all in any tipping, though we assumed it to be so. This is a contradiction. If it is not due to the addition of another condition, then preponderance without a preponderator would be necessary, which is impossible. So it is established that when that favor occurs, the action must occur, and when it is absent, it is impossible. And that returns to what we mentioned: that the occurrence of the action upon the combination of ability and motive is obligatory. That is exactly our doctrine.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The opponent asks: why cannot the term 'ja'l' (making) in the verse be interpreted as 'seeking divine favors' (al-alṭāf)? The author gives three counterarguments. First, the word 'ja'l' is specifically attached to 'Islam' in the verse, so diverting it to favors is a deviation. Second, according to the opponent's own principle, God does every possible favor, so asking for favors would be asking for what is already given, which is impossible. Third, the author provides a detailed logical argument: divine favors either have an effect in tipping the balance toward action or not. If they have no effect, they are irrelevant to faith. If they do have an effect, then once the tipping occurs, obligation results. He then analyzes the scenario where neither obligation nor impossibility follows, showing that either another condition intervenes (making the assumed favor ineffective) or there is preponderance without a preponderator (impossible). The conclusion is that the occurrence of the favor necessitates the action, and its absence prevents it—this is exactly the author's deterministic position that action is necessary given ability and motive.

Key Terms
ألطاف
ترجيح
وجوب
مانع
مرجح
قدرة
داعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse could be interpreted as seeking divine favors (al-alṭāf) without contradiction.
Correction: The author shows that this interpretation leads to absurdities: either asking for what is already given (second argument), or if favors effect action, then they necessitate obligation, which is the author's deterministic position.

Checkpoint
What is the third argument against interpreting 'ja'l' as seeking divine favors (al-alṭāf)?
A) The word 'ja'l' is specifically attached to Islam.
B) God has already done all possible favors.
C) If favors have an effect, they lead to obligation (wujūb).
D) Favors are not relevant to faith.

Key Takeaway
The third argument shows that any effective divine favor must necessitate action, confirming the author's deterministic view that ability plus motive makes action obligatory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the author's third argument—that divine favors must either be irrelevant or compel your actions—does it unsettle you? In your own life, do you believe there are moments when Allah's help (al-luṭf) leaves you no real choice but to obey? If so, how does that shape your sense of accountability for sins you commit after receiving such help?
?
The author compares ineffective favors to 'the croaking of a raven'—utterly irrelevant. Are there practices you thought were divine favors (like a spiritual feeling, a dream, or a sermon) that actually had no effect on your actions? If you stripped away those 'favors,' would your obedience or disobedience change? And if they do affect you, do they truly compel you, or do you still have room to resist?
?
The opponent's view assumes that divine favors can be 'sought' as if they are not already given. Think about a blessing (e.g., health, wealth, or a good environment) that you often ask for. Is it possible that Allah has already granted it in essence, yet you fail to act upon it? What would it mean for you to stop 'seeking' and start acting on what is already provided?

Defending the Strength of Intellectual Proofs
Defends that their intellectual proofs are stronger despite objections about praise and blame.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the strength of intellectual proofs for divine creation against an objection concerning praise and blame.

Core Idea
Asserting that one's proofs are stronger is a valid rebuttal when arguments have been previously established.

In this brief passage, the author encounters an objection: intellectual proofs suggest that actions cannot be created by God, which would seem to undermine the case for divine creation. The author responds confidently, reminding us that his own proofs have already been demonstrated to be stronger. This lesson explores the rhetorical and logical strategy of pointing to prior established arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Start by reading the objection embedded in the author's text. The objector claims that intellectual proofs indicate the impossibility of actions being created by God, and he locates this point within the discussion of praise and blame.
Notice that the objection is not fully explained; it is referenced concisely.
2
Understand the author's response
The author responds by stating that his own intellectual proofs are stronger and clearer, and he references that this was already established earlier. This shows that he relies on prior demonstrations rather than re-arguing them.
The phrase 'according to what was previously established' is key.
3
Reflect on the context
Think about why this exchange is important. The objection touches on the relationship between divine creation and human moral responsibility. The author's response assumes that the reader has followed the earlier arguments.
This passage is a transition, not a standalone proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله: «الدلائل العقلية دلت على امتناع وقوع الفعل بخلق الله تعالى وهي في فصل المدح والذم»، قلنا: دلائلنا العقلية أقوى وأجلى - على ما سبق تقريره

Commentary
This very brief passage records an objection and a concise response. The objector claims that intellectual proofs demonstrate that actions cannot be created by God – perhaps implying that human actions are entirely independent – and that this argument pertains to the domain of praise and blame (i.e., moral responsibility). The author simply counters by asserting that his own intellectual proofs are stronger and clearer, referencing that they have already been established earlier in the discussion. The brevity suggests that the author does not feel it necessary to repeat the arguments here, relying on prior demonstrations. The passage serves as a transition or a quick rebuttal within a larger debate.

The Principle of Causality and the Leaders of Misguidance
Author explains causality principle to answer objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the principle that an action is caused by power and motive resolves the objection about describing leaders of misguidance as 'imams'.

Core Idea
True causality lies in the combination of power and motive, not in direct divine creation of disbelief.

In this passage, the author addresses a subtle objection: if God creates all actions, how can we call someone an 'imam' in misguidance? He invokes the causal principle of power plus motive to show that the leaders' influence on motives is what earns them that description, without attributing the disbelief directly to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objection
Read the first part of the passage carefully. Notice that the objection is about the verse that mentions God making leaders of misguidance.
The author restates the first question before answering.
2
Understand the causal principle
The author states: 'action is caused by the combination of power with the motive.' Grasp that both power (qudra) and motive (dāʿī) are necessary for an action to occur.
This principle is key in Ash'ari thought.
3
Apply the principle to the leaders
See how the leaders' call influences the firmness of the motive in the heart, so they are described as imams due to their effective call, not because God directly created disbelief.
The leaders are not independent creators; they only affect motives.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider why this answer preserves God's justice. God provides power and motive, but the leaders' influence is what tips the scale, so they bear responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن هذه الآية التي تمسكتم بها ، ذكرها الله عقيب خبر آل فرعون . والجواب عن السؤال الأول : إنا إذا قلنا : الفعل معلول مجموع القدرة مع الداعي . فهذا السؤال زائل . وذلك لأن أئمة الضلال إذا دعوا إنسانا إلى الضلال وزينوه في قلبه . فدعوتهم هي التي أثرت في ترسخ تلك الداعية في قلب ذلك الإنسان . فلا جرم جاز وصفه بكونه إماما فيه . وأما تفسير الجعل بالحكم والتسمية . فقد تقدم جوابه . وأما حمله على أحوال القيامة : فالجواب عنه من وجهين : الأول : إن قوله : ( وجعلناهم ) لفظ للماضي ، فحمله على المستقبل خلاف الظاهر . والثاني : هب أن الأمر كما قالوه : إلا أنه تعالى لما أخبر عنهم أنهم يكونون كذلك في القيامة ، استحال أن لا يكونوا كذلك . وإلا لزم أن ينقلب خبر الله الذي يجب أن يكون صدقاً : كذباً . وذلك محال . والمفضي إلى المحال محال . وتمام تقريره ، قد تقدم . وبالله التوفيق
That this verse which you have clung to, Allah mentioned it after the news of the people of Pharaoh. And the answer to the first question: When we say: the action is caused by the combination of power with the motive, then this question vanishes. Because the leaders of misguidance, when they call a person to misguidance and make it seem attractive in his heart, their call is what influences the firmness of that motive in that person's heart. Therefore it is permissible to describe him as an imam in it. As for the interpretation of 'making' (jacl) as judgment and naming, its answer has preceded. And as for interpreting it as referring to the conditions of the Day of Resurrection, the answer is from two aspects: First: His statement 'And We made them' is a past tense verb, so interpreting it as future is contrary to the apparent meaning. Second: Suppose the matter is as they said, but since He informed about them that they will be like that on the Day of Resurrection, it is impossible that they not be like that. Otherwise, it would entail that the report of Allah, which must be truthful, would turn into falsehood, and that is impossible. And that which leads to the impossible is impossible. And its full elaboration has preceded. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is an answer to an objection. The author first identifies the verse in question—the one that appears after the story of Pharaoh. He then restates the first question and provides the answer based on the principle that an action is caused by the combination of power (qudra) and motive (dāʿī). According to this principle, the leaders of misguidance, by calling people to error and beautifying it in their hearts, influence the firmness of the motive in that person's heart. Hence, it is permissible to describe them as 'imāms' (leaders) in misguidance. The author then dismisses the interpretation of 'making' (jʿl) as merely judgment or naming, stating that its answer has already been given. Next, he addresses the interpretation that the verse refers to conditions on the Day of Resurrection. He gives two arguments: (1) The verb 'wa-jaʿalnāhum' (and We made them) is in the past tense, so interpreting it as future is against the apparent meaning. (2) Even if one assumes the interpretation is about the future, God's report about them being like that in the afterlife means it is impossible for them not to be so; otherwise, God's true report would become false, which is impossible. Whatever leads to the impossible is itself impossible.

Key Terms
الفعل معلول مجموع القدرة مع الداعي
أئمة الضلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that describing someone as an imam of misguidance implies God directly creates their disbelief.
Correction: The author clarifies that the leaders' call influences the motive, so they are imams due to their own effective call, not because God creates disbelief directly.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does describing the leaders of misguidance as 'imams' become permissible?
A) Because they have control over people's actions.
B) Because their call influences the motive in the heart.
C) Because God directly created them as imams.
D) Because the verse refers to a future event.

Key Takeaway
The leaders of misguidance are called imams because their influence strengthens the motive in the heart, not because God directly creates disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When someone around you beautifies a sin, have you felt your desire for it grow? What does this lesson reveal about your responsibility for the motives you nurture in your heart?
?
Are there people in your life whose call strengthens good motives in you, or do you find yourself more influenced by those who make wrong seem appealing? What will you do about that imbalance starting today?
?
If your actions depend on power and motive, then your motives are not neutral—they are being shaped by calls around you. Whose call are you listening to, and what does that say about the imams you are following?

Arguments Against the Future Resurrection Interpretation
Author gives two arguments against future interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two arguments the author gives against interpreting the verse as referring to conditions on the Day of Resurrection.

Core Idea
The past tense verb and the impossibility of God's report being false refute the future interpretation.

The author now tackles the claim that the verse 'And We made them' refers to the afterlife. He provides two compelling arguments: one based on the literal past tense, and the other on the logical necessity that God's truthful report cannot fail to come true.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Focus on the section that begins with 'As for interpreting it as referring to the conditions of the Day of Resurrection'.
The author first dismisses a secondary interpretation about 'judgment and naming' as already answered.
2
Understand the first argument: past tense
The verb 'jaʿalnāhum' is past tense, so interpreting it as future goes against the apparent meaning.
In Arabic, the past tense (māḍī) typically refers to completed past actions.
3
Understand the second argument: God's report must be true
Even if one assumes it refers to the future, God's information about them being like that on the Day of Resurrection makes it impossible for them not to be so. Otherwise, God's report would become false, which is impossible.
This argument leverages the attribute of truthfulness (ṣidq) of Allah.

God Creates Faith and Disbelief from His Attribute 'Doer of What He Wills'
Proof from God's will for creating faith and disbelief, with objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari argument from the attribute 'Doer of what He wills' establishes that God creates both faith and disbelief, and how the objection limiting the phrase to God's own acts is refuted.

Core Idea
God's attribute 'Doer of what He wills' includes creating human acts of faith and disbelief.

This passage presents a key argument in the debate about divine causation of human actions. The author begins with the agreed premise that God wills faith, and argues that since He wills it, He must be its doer. He then extends this to disbelief, and answers an objection that attempts to restrict the scope of the attribute 'Doer of what He wills'.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting the logical flow from premise to conclusion and the objection-response structure.
The key phrase is 'فعال لما يريد'.
2
Identify the main argument
Find the author's claim that God is the doer of the believer's faith based on the text, and his extension to disbelief.
Notice the phrase 'ضرورة أنه لا قائل بالفرق'.
3
Understand the objection
The opponent says the phrase means God does His own acts, not the acts of servants. Recognize that this objection challenges the applicability of the argument.
The objection starts with 'فإن قيل'.
4
Analyze the author's response
The author argues that the phrase includes everything God wills to exist, and the opponent's interpretation goes against the apparent meaning.
The response uses 'قلنا'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يفعل ما يريد ولا نزاع أنه تعالى أراد الإيمان. أما عندكم فمن الكل. وأما عندنا فمن المؤمن . فوجب أن يكون هو الفاعل لإيمانه ، بحكم هذا النص وإذا ثبت هذا ، ثبت أنه هو الفاعل للكفر. ضرورة أنه لا قائل بالفرق . فإن قيل : المراد أنه « فعال لما يريد ، فعله . فلم قلتم : إنه تعالى يريد أن يفعل فعل العبد ، فإن ذلك أول المسألة ؟ قلنا : قوله تعالى : ( فعال لما يريد ) يتناول كل ما يريد وجوده ، وما ذكرتموه يفيد . خلاف الظاهر
He does what He wills, and there is no dispute that the Most High willed faith. As for you (plural), it is from all (people). And as for us, it is from the believer. So it is necessary that He is the doer of his faith, by virtue of this text. And if this is established, it is established that He is the doer of disbelief, necessarily because there is no one who distinguishes between them. If it is said: What is meant is that He is 'Doer of what He wills', so why do you say that the Most High wills to do the act of the servant, for that is the beginning of the issue? We say: His statement, the Most High: 'Doer of what He wills' encompasses everything He wills to exist, and what you mentioned gives a meaning contrary to the apparent (meaning).
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that God creates both faith and disbelief. The author argues from the general principle that God does what He wills and that He willed faith. Since He willed it, He must be the doer of it. Then by extension, He is the doer of disbelief as well, because no one distinguishes between the two. The objection attempts to limit the phrase 'Doer of what He wills' to God's own acts, not the acts of servants. The answer affirms that the phrase includes everything God wills to exist, including the acts of servants, and that the opponent's interpretation goes against the apparent meaning of the text.

Key Terms
إيمان
كفر
فعال لما يريد
فاعل
نص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'Doer of what He wills' only refers to God's direct acts, not to His creation of human actions.
Correction: The phrase encompasses everything God wills to exist, including the acts of servants.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the objection in this passage?
A) To argue that God does not will disbelief.
B) To limit the phrase 'Doer of what He wills' to God's own actions, excluding the acts of servants.
C) To prove that God creates faith but not disbelief.
D) To claim that the text is not authentic.

Key Takeaway
God's attribute 'Doer of what He wills' establishes that He creates both faith and disbelief, as it encompasses everything He wills to exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The objection in the passage tries to limit God's will to His own acts, not yours. Have you ever secretly thought that your good deeds are your own achievement? How does this verse strip you of that pride and force you to rely entirely on His creation of faith within you?
?
God is the doer of disbelief as well as faith. If you ever despise a disbeliever for his unbelief, remember that the same hand that sustains your faith willed his disbelief. How should this reshape your compassion for those who reject truth—and your urgency in supplication for them?

Fifth Proof: 'Be and It Is' as Evidence for Divine Creation of Faith
The author cites the verse of 'Be and it is' to prove that God creates faith and all obedience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Qur'anic verse 'Be and it is' is used to prove that God creates faith and all human acts of obedience.

Core Idea
God's command 'Be' applies to faith just as it does to all creation.

This lesson presents the fifth proof from the text, which uses a powerful Qur'anic verse to establish that God is the creator of human faith. The verse 'Be and it is' demonstrates that everything God wills comes into existence simply by His command. Applying this to faith shows that it is not a human product but a divine creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own flow come through.
Notice the verse citation and how the author immediately applies it.
2
Understand the verse 'Be and it is'
Reflect on the meaning of the verse: when God intends something, He simply says 'Be' and it becomes. This shows God's absolute power over existence.
This verse is often cited in discussions of divine omnipotence.
3
See the author's application
The author applies the verse to faith: God willed faith, so it must have come into being by His command. Then extends it to all acts of obedience.
The key move is from general principle to specific case.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The conclusion is that faith and all obedience are created by God's act of bringing into existence (takwin and ijad).
This counters any idea that humans independently produce their own faith.
5
Address the common misconception
Some think this verse only applies to physical creation. The author argues it applies to all things God wills, including faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : قوله تعالى : ( إنما قولنا لشيء ، إذا أردناه . أن نقول له : كن فيكون (۲) ) وقد أراد الله الإيمان، فوجب أن يتكون ذلك الإيمان بقوله : ( كن فيكون ) وذلك عبارة عن تكوين الله إياه . فدل هذا النص : على أن إيمان العبد ، وجميع طاعاته ، حصل بتكوين الله وإيجاده
The Fifth Proof: His statement, the Most High: 'Our saying to a thing, when We intend it, is to say to it: Be, and it becomes' (Surah an-Nahl, verse 40). And God willed faith, so it is necessary that that faith comes into being by His saying: 'Be, and it becomes,' and that is an expression of God's bringing it into being. So this text indicates that the faith of the servant and all his acts of obedience occurred by God's bringing into existence and creation.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage uses a Quranic verse to prove that God creates faith. The verse states that God's command 'Be' brings any thing into existence. Since God willed faith to exist, it must have been created by His command. The conclusion extends this to all acts of obedience, affirming that they are brought into being by God.

Key Terms
قولنا
كن فيكون
تكوين
إيجاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'Be and it is' only applies to physical creation, not to human moral or spiritual acts like faith.
Correction: The author argues that the verse is universal: whatever God wills, including faith, comes into being by His command. Thus faith is a divine creation, not a human product.

Checkpoint
What does the verse 'Be and it is' demonstrate according to this passage?
A) That God creates only inanimate objects.
B) That faith is created by God's command just like any other thing.
C) That humans create their own faith.
D) That the verse is metaphorical.

Key Takeaway
The Qur'anic verse 'Be and it is' proves that faith and all acts of obedience are directly created by God's command.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you struggle to obey Allah, do you truly believe that your very will to obey is created by His command 'Be'? How would your effort change if you relied on His creative power rather than your own resolve?
?
If faith is directly created by God's command, what does that mean for your pride in your own belief? Are you willing to acknowledge that your iman exists solely because Allah said 'Be' to it at every moment?
?
Consider a sin you committed. Did you think you were acting independently of Allah's creation? How does this verse reshape your understanding of accountability and reliance on Allah even for turning away from sin?

Sixth Proof: God Creates Heedlessness – Quranic Argument and Objection
The author proves from the verb pattern that God creates heedlessness, then refutes an objection with linguistic and rational arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quranic verb ‘أغفلنا’ is used to prove that God creates heedlessness, and how scholars refute the objection that it means ‘found’ rather than ‘made’.

Core Idea
The verb ‘أغفلنا’ is proof that God creates heedlessness, not that He merely finds it.

This lesson presents the sixth proof from the Quran, which argues that the verb pattern of ‘أغفلنا’ implies creation of heedlessness, similar to ‘أحينا وأمتنا’ for life and death. An objection is raised that it could mean ‘found’, but the answer provides both linguistic and rational arguments to uphold the creation interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof
Read the first part of the passage where the author presents the proof: the verb ‘أغفلنا’ is on the same pattern as ‘أحينا وأمتنا’, so it must indicate creation of heedlessness just as those verbs indicate creation of life and death.
Notice the analogy: just as ‘أحينا’ means ‘We gave life’, ‘أغفلنا’ means ‘We made heedless’.
2
Understand the objection
The objection claims that ‘أغفلنا’ might mean ‘We found his heart heedless’ rather than ‘We made it heedless’. It cites a poetic usage of similar verb forms to support this interpretation.
The objection also argues that if ‘made’ were intended, the verse would use ‘fa’ (so) to indicate compliance, not ‘wa’ (and).
3
Follow the first part of the answer
The author gives three linguistic reasons why the literal meaning is ‘creation’ (takwin): frequency, immediate understanding, and the rational priority of the original meaning over the derived.
Each argument reinforces that ‘takwin’ is the primary, literal sense.
4
Follow the second part of the answer
The author then gives a rational argument: the servant cannot create his own heedlessness because he cannot intend it without prior knowledge, leading to an infinite regress. Therefore, God must create it.
This part shows the theological necessity of the creation interpretation.
5
Reflect and check understanding
Review the entire argument: proof, objection, two-part answer. Ask yourself why the objection fails and how the answer upholds the creation of heedlessness by God.
Consider how this fits into the broader topic of divine causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : قوله تعالى : ( ولا تطع من أغفلنا قلبه عن ذكرنا (۳) : وذلك لأن قوله : ( أغفلنا ) على وزن قوله : أحينا وأمتنا . فكما أن هذا يفيد خلق الحياة ، فكذا قوله : ( أغفلنا ) وجب أن يفيد خلق الغفلة فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : المراد بقوله : ( أغفلنا قلبه عن ذكرنا ) إنا وجدنا قلبه غافلا . ولا يكون المراد منه : خلق الغفلة . والدليل عليه : ما روي عن عمرو بن معدي كرب الزبيدي، أنه قال لبني سليم : « قاتلناكم فيا أجبناكم ، وسألناكم فما أبخلناكم ، وهجـوناكم فما أفحمناكم ، أي : ما وجدناكم جبناء ، ولا بخلاء ، ولا مفحمين ثم نقول : حمل هذا اللفظ ، على هذا المعنى : أولى . والدليل عليه : أنه لو كان المراد ، هو أنه تعالى جعل قلبه ،غافلاً ، لوجب أن يقال : ولا تطع أغفلنا قلبه عن ذكرنا . فاتبع هواه . لأن هذا على هذا التقدير يكون من أفعال المطاوعة . وهي إنما تعطف بالفاء، لا بالواو . ومنه قولهم : كسرته فانكسر ، ودفعته فاندفع ، ولا يقال : وانكسر واندفع والجواب عن الأول من وجهين : الأول : إن الاشتراك خلاف الأصل، فوجب أن يعتقد أن وزن الأفعال حقيقة في أحدها ، مجاز في الآخر. وجعله حقيقة في التكوين ، مجازاً في الوجدان : أولى من العكس . ويدل عليه وجوه : الوجه الأول : إن مجيء هذا البناء بمعنى التكوين أكثر من مجيئه بمعنى الوجدان . والكثرة دليل الرجمحان .. الثاني : إن مبادرة الفهم إلى التكوين ، أكثر من مبادرته إلى الوجدان . أعني : من هذا اللفظ ، ومبادرة الفهم دليل كونه حقيقة فيه الثالث : إنا إن جعلناه حقيقة في التكوين ، أمكن جعله مجازاً عن الوجدان ، لأن العلم بالشيء تابع لحصول المعلوم ، فجعل اللفظ حقيقة في الأصل، مجازاً في التبع ، موافق للعقول . أما لو جعلناه حقيقة في الوجدان ، مجازاً في الوجود، لزم جعله حقيقة في التبع ، مجازاً في الأصل ، وإنه على خلاف المعقول والوجه الثاني في الجواب : إنا نسلم كون اللفظ ، بالنسبة إلى هذين المفهومين . إلا أنا نقول : يجب حمل قوله : ( أغفلنا ) على خلق الغفلة . وذلك لأن العبد يمتنع كونه موجداً لغفلة نفسه. والدليل عليه : أنه إذا حاول إيجاد الغفلة. فإما أن يحاول إيجاد مطلق الغفلة ، أو يحاول إيجاد الغفلة عن شيء معين . والأول باطل . وإلا لم يكن بأن تحصل له الغفلة عن هذا أولى من أن تحصل له الغفلة عن ذاك . لأن الطبيعة الحسنة ، نسبتها إلى كل الأنواع على السوية . وأما الثاني - فهو أيضاً محال . لأن الغفلة عن كذا عبارة عن غفلة لا تمتاز عن سائر الغفلات ، إلا بكونها منتسبة إلى ذلك الشيء المعين ، فلا يمكنه أن يقصد إلى إيقاع الغفلة عن كذا ، إلا إذا اعتقد كونه غفلة عن كذا . ولا يمكنه أن يعتقد كون هذا الاعتقاد غفلة عن كذا ، لا إذا تصور ذلك الشيء . لأن العلم بنسبة أمر إلى أمر ، مشروط بتصور كل واحد من المنتسبين
The Sixth Proof: His statement, the Most High: 'And do not obey one whose heart We have made heedless of Our remembrance' (Surah al-Kahf, verse 28). This is because His word 'أغفلنا' is on the pattern of His word 'أحينا وأمتنا'. So just as this indicates the creation of life, so too His word 'أغفلنا' must indicate the creation of heedlessness. If it is said: Why is it not permissible to say that the meaning of His word 'We made his heart heedless' is that We found his heart heedless, and the intended meaning is not the creation of heedlessness? The evidence for this is what was narrated from 'Amr ibn Ma'dikarib az-Zubaydi, that he said to Bani Sulaym: 'We fought you and [did not find you cowardly]? Actually the quote is: We fought you, so we found you not cowardly? (فما أجبناكم) — the text is unclear. And we asked you, so we did not find you stingy. And we satirized you, so we did not find you silenced. i.e., we did not find you cowardly, nor stingy, nor silenced.' Then we say: Carrying this word to this meaning is more appropriate. The evidence for it: that if the intended meaning was that the Most High made his heart heedless, then it would be necessary to say: 'And do not obey one whose heart We have made heedless, so he follows his desire.' Because this, on this assumption, would be an act of compliance (mutawa'a). And acts of compliance are only conjoined with فا, not with واو. An example is their saying: 'I broke it so it broke' (kasar-tuhu fankasar), and 'I pushed it so it was pushed' (dafa'tuhu fandafa'a), and it is not said 'wa-nkasara' or 'wa-ndafa'a'. The answer to the first (objection) is from two aspects: The first: that polysemy (ishtirak) is contrary to the principle, so it is necessary to believe that the verb pattern is literal in one of them and metaphorical in the other. And making it literal in the sense of creation (takwin) and metaphorical in the sense of finding (wijdan) is preferable to the reverse. This is supported by three points: The first: that the occurrence of this pattern with the meaning of creation is more frequent than its occurrence with the meaning of finding, and frequency is evidence of preponderance. The second: that the immediate understanding (mubadarat al-fahm) tends toward creation more than toward finding—i.e., from this word—and immediate understanding is evidence that it is literal in that sense. The third: if we make it literal in creation, we can make it metaphorical for finding, because knowledge of something is consequent upon the existence of the known. So making the word literal in the origin (creation) and metaphorical in the derivative (finding) is consistent with reason. But if we made it literal in finding and metaphorical in existence, we would be making it literal in the derivative and metaphorical in the origin, which is contrary to reason. The second aspect in the answer: we concede that the word (in itself) relates to these two concepts, but we say: it is necessary to interpret His word 'أغفلنا' as the creation of heedlessness. This is because the servant cannot be the creator of his own heedlessness. The evidence is: if he tries to create heedlessness, he either tries to create absolute heedlessness or tries to create heedlessness of a particular thing. The first is invalid, because otherwise he would have no more reason to have heedlessness of this thing rather than that, since the human nature (al-tabi'a al-hasana) is equally related to all types. As for the second, it is also impossible, because heedlessness of something is a heedlessness that is not distinguished from other heedlessnesses except by its relation to that particular thing. So he cannot intend to bring about heedlessness of something unless he believes it to be heedlessness of that thing. And he cannot believe that this belief is heedlessness of that thing unless he imagines that thing, because knowledge of the relation of one thing to another is conditional upon imagining each of the two related items.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth proof, an objection, and a detailed answer. The proof argues from the Quranic verb form 'أغفلنا' that God creates heedlessness, by analogy with 'أحينا وأمتنا' which clearly mean creating life and death. The objection claims that the verb might mean 'found' not 'made', citing a poetic usage. The answer has two parts. First, it uses linguistic principles: the pattern is more commonly used for creation, immediate understanding favors creation, and it is rational to treat the more fundamental meaning (creation) as literal. Second, it gives a rational argument: the servant cannot create his own heedlessness because he cannot intend it without already knowing it, leading to an infinite regress. Thus, the verse must mean God creates heedlessness.

Key Terms
أغفلنا
أحينا وأمتنا
غفلة
وجدان
تكوين
اشتراك
حقيقة
مجاز
مطاوعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verb form 'af’ala' always means 'to find' rather than 'to make' in Arabic, based on select poetic examples.
Correction: Frequency and immediate understanding show that the primary meaning is 'to make' (creation), and the secondary meaning 'to find' is metaphorical. Moreover, the rational argument shows that only God can create heedlessness.

Checkpoint
What is the main weakness the answer points out in the objection's interpretation of 'أغفلنا' as 'We found'?
A) The word is never used in that way.
B) The servant cannot create his own heedlessness, making the 'creation' interpretation necessary.
C) The grammar of the verse requires a different reading.
D) The objection relies on a weak hadith.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic verb ‘أغفلنا’ must be understood as God creating heedlessness, not merely finding it heedless, based on frequency, immediate understanding, and the rational impossibility of self-creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you catch yourself being heedless of Allah, do you truly believe that this heedlessness was created by Him? How does that belief change your response to it—are you driven to repentance or to excuse?
?
If your own heedlessness is created by God, then what prevents you from turning to Him in complete reliance, begging Him to remove it? Why do you still rely on your own willpower despite knowing this truth?
?
How many times have you attributed your forgetfulness of Allah to your own weakness or circumstances, without once realizing that the very moment of heedlessness was a direct creation from your Lord? Will you now, every time you become heedless, immediately confess that He is the Creator of that state and seek refuge in Him?
