The Ninth Proof: Victory from Allah
Al-Razi presents the ninth proof from the Qur'an, defining victory and refuting an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi uses the Qur'anic verse on victory to argue that all human actions are created by Allah, and how he addresses the objection that victory is purely divine aid.

Core Idea
Victory is from Allah, so the actions that achieve victory must also be from Allah.

Al-Razi continues his series of Qur'anic proofs. In this ninth proof, he draws on a verse that attributes victory solely to Allah. He unpacks what victory actually entails—bodily and intellectual actions—and argues that since these actions are part of victory, and victory is from Allah, the actions themselves are created by Allah. An important objection about divine intervention is then addressed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then the English translation. Notice how the proof is structured: verse, analysis, conclusion, objection, response.
The passage is a complete argument with a clear objection and answer.
2
Identify the two types of victory
Al-Razi distinguishes between bodily actions (e.g., wrestling) and intellectual actions (e.g., argument). Both are actions of the servant. Notice that he says 'victory means nothing other than that'.
This categorization is crucial for his argument.
3
Connect the verse to human actions
Since the verse says victory is only from Allah, and victory consists of these human actions, then those actions must be from Allah. That is the core inference: the action of the servant is produced by Allah's origination.
This links divine causation directly to human acts.
4
Examine the objection and response
The objection says victory might be only divine aid (strengthening heart, sending angels). Al-Razi replies that even if that is called victory, the effective actions that cause overcoming are also called victory, so the verse covers both. Thus the original conclusion holds.
Al-Razi uses a linguistic argument about the scope of the term 'victory'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة : قوله تعالى : ( وما النصر إلا من عند الله ) ومعلوم ان كون الإنسان منصوراً على الغير، إما أن يكون بأفعال الجوارح . كما إذا تصارع رجلان ، ثم إن أحدهما يصرع الآخر ، أو بالعلم واللسان ، كما إذا أغلب أحدهما صاحبه بالحجة والبرهان . فإذا كان النصر لا معنى له إلا ذلك وكل ذلك فعل العبد ، ثم دل هذا النص، على أن النصر ليس إلا من الله ، ثبت : أن فعل العبد حاصل بإيجاد الله فإن قيل : النصر قد يكون بتقوية القلب ، وبالإمداد بالملائكة ، وبإزالة الخوف عن القلب . وكل ذلك من الله . قلنا : هب أن ما ذكرتموه يسمى بالنصر ، إلا أن الأفعال المؤثرة في الغلبة ، لا شك أنها أيضاً مسماة بالنصر . فلما قال تعالى : ( وما النصر إلا من عند الله ) دخل فيه ما ذكرتم وما ذكرنا . وحينئذ يحصل المطلوب
The ninth proof: His statement, the Exalted: (And victory is only from Allah). It is known that a person’s being victorious over another is either through bodily actions, as when two men wrestle and one overthrows the other, or through knowledge and speech, as when one overcomes his companion with proof and evidence. So if victory means nothing other than that, and all of that is the action of the servant, then this text indicates that victory is only from Allah, so it is established: that the action of the servant is produced by Allah’s origination. If it is said: Victory may be through strengthening the heart, supplying angels, and removing fear from the heart, and all that is from Allah. We say: Suppose that what you mention is called victory, but the actions effective in overcoming are undoubtedly also called victory. So when the Exalted said: (And victory is only from Allah), it includes what you mentioned and what we mentioned. And then the desired conclusion is reached.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is a Qur'anic proof that human actions are created by Allah. Al-Razi presents the ninth proof based on the verse 'And victory is only from Allah.' He argues that victory over an opponent occurs through bodily actions (like wrestling) or intellectual actions (like argument). Both are actions of the servant. If all victory is from Allah, as the verse states, then these actions must be originated by Allah. An objection is raised: victory could mean divine aid like strengthening the heart or sending angels. Al-Razi responds that even if that is called victory, the physical and intellectual actions that cause victory are also called victory. Thus the verse encompasses both, proving that all such actions are from Allah.

Key Terms
نصر
فعل العبد
إيجاد الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That this proof only refers to non-human actions like divine aid, ignoring the human actions involved in victory.
Correction: Al-Razi clarifies that victory encompasses both divine aid and human actions, so the verse applies to both.

Checkpoint
What does the ninth proof conclude about human actions?
A) They are independent of Allah's creation.
B) They are created by Allah because victory, which involves them, is from Allah.
C) They are only metaphors for divine aid.
D) They are caused by angels.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi shows that the verse 'Victory is only from Allah' logically implies that the actions through which victory is achieved are also created by Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you achieve something you worked hard for, do you genuinely attribute the victory to Allah's creation of your actions, or do you silently credit your own effort? What would it mean to you to truly believe that every movement and thought you made was created by Him alone?
?
Al-Razi mentions winning an argument through proof and evidence. The next time you 'win' a discussion or debate, pause and ask: Was your articulation of arguments truly from you, or did Allah create that ability in that moment? How would that shift your humility and gratitude?
?
If you accepted that Allah creates even your failures and successes, would your fear of failing or your pride in succeeding dissolve? Today, can you commit to repeating 'And victory is only from Allah' before any important task, and then act with complete reliance on Him?

The Eleventh Proof: Expansion of the Chest
Al-Razi presents a Qur'anic proof with objection and two answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi uses the Qur'anic concept of 'expansion of the chest' to argue that all knowledge is created directly by Allah.

Core Idea
The Qur'anic 'expansion of the chest' proves knowledge is a direct creation of Allah.

Al-Razi presents his eleventh proof, drawing on three key Qur'anic verses that speak of Allah expanding the chest. He interprets this expansion not as mere emotional relief, but as the very creation of knowledge and understanding within the heart. This short but dense passage includes both his positive argument and his response to an alternative interpretation, showing how careful textual analysis supports the Ash'ari doctrine of divine creation of all acts, including human cognition.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the full Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of argument.
Notice the three Qur'anic verses cited.
2
Identify the proof's core claim
Find where Al-Razi states the meaning of 'expansion of the chest' and how he uses it to conclude that knowledge is created by Allah.
3
Understand the objection
Read the objection that suggests expansion means removal of fear and grief. Note how it challenges the proof.
4
Follow Al-Razi's two answers
Both answers are given to refute the objection. The first answer is based on timing, the second on the specific wording of a verse.
These answers are distinct and sequential.
5
Connect to the larger lesson
Reflect on how this proof supports the overall theme that all cognitive acts are directly created by Allah, not by human agency.
This connects to the broader kalam discussion on causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الحادية عشرة : قوله تعالى : ( رب اشرح لي صدري ) وقوله : ( الم نشرح لك صدرك ؟ ) وقوله : ( أفمن شرح الله صدره للإسلام ) ولا معنى لانشراح الصدر ، إلا حصول العلوم والمعارف . فهذه النصوص كلها دالة على أن المعارف والعلوم إنما تحصل بخلق الله تعالى فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يكون المراد من شرح الصدر ، إزالة الخوف والغم والحزن عنه ، بحيث لا يتأذى بكل ما يسمع من المكروهات ؟ فالجواب عنه من وجهين : الأول : إن كل ما يتعلق بالإقدار والتمكين ، وإزالة الموانع والعلل ، كان حاصلا قبل أن قال موسى - عليه السلام - : ( رب اشرح لي صدري ) فيمتنع حمل هذا السؤال على تلك الأشياء الثاني : قيد هذا الشرح بالإسلام . فقال : ( أفمن شرح الله صدره للإسلام ) ثم قال : ( فهو على نور من ربه ) وذلك النور ليس إلا المعرفة . والله أعلم
The eleventh proof: His statement, the Exalted: (My Lord, expand for me my breast.) And His statement: (Did We not expand for you your breast?) And His statement: (Then is one whose breast Allah has expanded for Islam...) There is no meaning for expansion of the breast except the attainment of sciences and cognitions. So all these texts indicate that cognitions and sciences are only attained by the creation of Allah, the Exalted. If it is said: Why is it not permissible that what is meant by expansion of the breast is the removal of fear, grief, and sorrow from him, so that he is not harmed by whatever he hears of unpleasant things? The answer is in two ways: First, everything related to enabling, empowerment, and removal of obstacles and defects was already present before Moses - peace be upon him - said: (My Lord, expand for me my breast.) So it is impossible to interpret this request as referring to those things. Second, He qualified this expansion with Islam. He said: (Then is one whose breast Allah has expanded for Islam) then He said: (so he is upon a light from his Lord) and that light is nothing but cognition. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is a Qur'anic proof that knowledge is created by Allah. Al-Razi cites three verses about 'expansion of the chest' (sharh al-sadr). He argues that expansion means acquisition of knowledge. Therefore, since these verses attribute expansion to Allah, knowledge is created by Him. An objection suggests expansion refers to removal of fear and grief. Al-Razi gives two answers: First, removal of obstacles was already present before Moses' prayer, so the prayer must be for something else. Second, the verse in Surat al-Zumar explicitly links expansion to Islam and a light from Allah, which is cognition. Thus the interpretation as knowledge is confirmed.

Key Terms
شرح الصدر
المعارف
العلوم
خلق الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Qur'anic 'expansion of the chest' refers only to emotional relief or removal of fear and sorrow.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that expansion means acquisition of knowledge and cognition, based on the textual context (especially the 'light from his Lord' in Surah al-Zumar) and the timing of Moses' prayer.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Razi's eleventh proof, what is the meaning of 'expansion of the chest' (sharh al-sadr)?
A) Removing fear and sorrow.
B) Gaining physical strength.
C) Acquiring knowledge and cognition.
D) Receiving wealth.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi uses the Qur'anic concept of 'expansion of the chest' to demonstrate that all knowledge is directly created by Allah, not acquired through independent human effort.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself struggling to understand a concept in your faith, do you genuinely ask Allah to 'expand your chest' with knowledge, or do you rely on your own efforts and feel frustrated? What would change if you truly believed that every insight is a direct creation from Him?
?
Moses already had empowerment and removal of obstacles, yet he still asked for expansion. Is there an area in your life where you have the capacity and means but lack deep understanding? Are you avoiding asking Allah for that knowledge because it would demand greater responsibility?
?
The objection in this lesson reduces 'expansion' to mere emotional relief. In your personal du'as, do you often ask for comfort and ease rather than for true cognition and guidance? What might be the cost of prioritizing emotional comfort over the light of knowledge?

The Twelfth Proof: Ease of Affairs after Expansion
Al-Razi's proof from the sequence of Moses' request for ease.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi uses the sequence of Moses' prayer to prove that Allah creates knowledge and obedience.

Core Idea
Prayer for ease implies creation of knowledge and obedience, not just ability.

Al-Razi presents a subtle linguistic and theological proof from the Qur'anic narrative of Prophet Moses. By examining the progression of Moses' request—from expansion of the chest to ease of affairs—Al-Razi argues that the 'ease' must be a divine act of creating knowledge and obedience, not merely removing obstacles. This passage demonstrates the kalam method of interpreting ambiguous commands through context.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage from Al-Razi carefully. Notice the flow: he quotes the verse, then states a general principle, then eliminates an alternative interpretation, then concludes.
2
Understand the Qur'anic context
The passage refers to Moses' prayer in Surah Ta-Ha (20:25-28). Al-Razi quotes two consecutive phrases: 'expand my breast' and 'ease my affair.' Recognize the sequence.
3
Analyze the argument
Follow Al-Razi's reasoning: since Moses already had the ability and obstacles were removed, the request for 'ease' must be for something else—namely, the creation of knowledge and obedience. This is the core of the proof.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider why this matters: it supports the Ash'ari position that all actions, including knowledge and obedience, are created directly by Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية عشر : أنه كما قال أولاً : ( رب اشرح لي صدري ) قال بعده : ( ويسر لي أمري ) وهذا الأمر مجمل، فوجب حمله على ما تقدم ذكره ، وهو قيامه بتبليغ الرسالة ، وشروعه في تلك المهمات على الوجه الأبلغ الأحسن . ولا يمكن حمل هذا التيسير على الإقدار ، وإزاحة العلل والأعذار . لان كل ذلك ، كان حاصلا قبل هذا الدعاء. فوجب حمل هذا السؤال على خلق المعرفة والطاعة
The twelfth proof: That just as he said first: (My Lord, expand for me my breast) he said after it: (and ease for me my affair.) And this command is general, so it must be interpreted as what was previously mentioned, which is his undertaking to convey the message and his commencing in those important matters in the most complete and best manner. It is not possible to interpret this easing as enabling or removal of obstacles and excuses, because all that was present before this supplication. So it is necessary to interpret this request as the creation of knowledge and obedience.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage is a Qur'anic proof from the sequence of Moses' prayer. Al-Razi notes that after asking for expansion, Moses asks for ease of his affair. The 'affair' is his mission of conveying the message. Easing cannot mean just giving ability because that was already present. Therefore, the prayer must be for the creation of knowledge and obedience, showing that these are from Allah.

Correction: Al-Razi argues that ability and removal of obstacles were already present, so 'ease' must refer to the creation of knowledge and obedience, which are internal acts of Allah.

Checkpoint
Why does Al-Razi reject the interpretation that 'ease' means giving ability?
A) Because ability was already present before the prayer.
B) Because ability is not mentioned in the verse.
C) Because Moses was already able.
D) Because ease must be a future event.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's proof from the sequence of Moses' prayer shows that 'ease' must refer to Allah's creation of knowledge and obedience, not mere ability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray for ease in a difficult task, do you truly believe that Allah must create knowledge and obedience in your heart, or do you assume you already have ability and merely need external obstacles removed? What does your prayer actually expect from Allah?
?
Moses, the greatest prophet, asked for the creation of knowledge and obedience. When you face a daunting responsibility, do you ask Allah to create in you the very capacity to obey and understand? Or do you rely on your own preparation? This week, before a challenge, pray specifically for Allah to create knowledge and obedience in you, not just for circumstances to improve.
?
If ease is only possible through Allah's direct creation of knowledge and obedience, then what does that mean for your daily actions? Are you living as though your good deeds are your own, or do you constantly attribute them to Allah's creation? How would your humility change if you truly internalized this lesson?

The Thirteenth Proof: Supplication Against Deviation
Al-Razi introduces the verse and begins the first argument aspect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the verse 'Do not cause our hearts to deviate' forms the basis of a rational argument about divine causality, and why the supplication implies that Allah can cause deviation.

Core Idea
The prayer against deviation presupposes that Allah is the one who could cause it.

Al-Razi begins his thirteenth proof by citing a familiar Qur'anic supplication. The verse asks Allah not to cause our hearts to stray after guidance. At first glance, this seems like a simple prayer, but Al-Razi draws a profound theological implication: if Allah never caused deviation, the prayer would be meaningless. This lesson explores the opening of that argument, even though the full proof is incomplete.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and its context
Start by reading the Arabic verse and its translation. Notice that it is a supplication: 'Our Lord, do not cause our hearts to deviate after You have guided us...' Reflect on why believers would ask Allah specifically not to cause deviation.
The verse is from Surah Aal-e-Imran (3:8).
2
Identify the two aspects of the argument
Al-Razi states that the argument from this verse has two aspects. The first aspect is partially given: if Allah never causes deviation at all, then the supplication would be incoherent. Pause and think about why that is the case.
The passage cuts off before the second aspect is mentioned.
3
Understand the first aspect's logic
The first aspect works by contradiction: the verse assumes it is possible for Allah to cause deviation, otherwise the prayer would be pointless. This implies that deviation is within divine power.
4
Acknowledge the incomplete argument
Recognize that the passage ends abruptly. The second aspect and the full conclusion are not given. This lesson only covers what is available, but the direction is clear: the verse supports the idea that Allah is the agent of guidance and misguidance.
In later lessons, the full proof will be completed.

Source Text

The thirteenth proof: His statement, the Exalted: (Our Lord, do not cause our hearts to deviate after You have guided us, and grant us from Yourself mercy.) Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage begins the thirteenth Qur'anic proof, but it is incomplete due to page break. Al-Razi introduces the verse asking Allah not to cause deviation. He states that the argument has two aspects, but only the first is partially given: if Allah never causes deviation, then the prayer would be meaningless. The passage cuts off before the conclusion. The incomplete nature means we cannot fully analyze the argument.

Key Takeaway
The supplication 'Do not cause our hearts to deviate' builds on the assumption that Allah can indeed cause deviation, making the prayer meaningful.

Interpretation of 'Rushd' Given to Abraham
Argues that 'rushd' given to Abraham denotes acquired knowledge and actions, proving acts occur by God's creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term 'rushd' (right guidance) in Abraham's story is interpreted by the author to support the doctrine that human actions are created by God.

Core Idea
The 'rushd' given to Abraham is not mere intellect but divinely bestowed knowledge and actions.

In this passage, the author analyzes the Quranic term 'rushd' bestowed upon Abraham. He argues that this cannot refer to basic intellect or general guidance, as these are common to all. Instead, it must refer to the abundant knowledge and noble actions granted to Abraham, which in turn proves that all human actions are created by God. This lesson will walk through the author's reasoning and his responses to potential objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the flow: the author first eliminates possible meanings, then responds to objections.
The passage contains two objections and answers; follow the argument closely.
2
Identify the author's narrowing of 'rushd'
The author rejects three possible meanings: basic intellect/soundness, divine success/elucidation, and later prophethood. Identify why each is rejected.
The key verse is 'And We were knowing of him'—this links rushd to God's special knowledge.
3
Understand the first objection (prophethood) and response
An objector suggests rushd means prophethood. The author counters that the word 'rushd' is absolute and restricting it to prophethood is contrary to apparent meaning.
Notice the author uses the absolute nature of the term to argue against restriction.
4
Understand the second objection (intellect) and response
Another objector suggests rushd means intellect and complete understanding. The author responds that intellect is universal, but rushd is only for those who use it beneficially.
The author cites the Quranic verse about giving wealth upon reaching 'rushd' to show it means sound judgment, not mere intellect.
5
Grasp the final conclusion and doctrinal implication
The author concludes that rushd must refer to the abundant knowledge and noble actions granted uniquely to Abraham, which demonstrates that human actions are created by God.
This conclusion directly supports the Ash'ari position on divine creation of human acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكنا به عالمين وإيتاء الرشد، ليس عبارة عن أصل العقل وسلامة المزاج لأن ذلك حاصل في حق الأكثرين. فلا يجوز ذكره في معرض المنة عليه. وليس المراد أيضاً: التوفيق والبيان. لأن كل ذلك حاصل في حق الكفار. فوجب أن يكون المراد: ما حصل عنده من العلوم الكثيرة والأفعال الشريفة. وذلك يدل على أن أفعال العبد تحصل بخلق الله تعالى.
فإن قيل: لم لا يجوز أن يكون المراد من الرشد: النبوة؟ والدليل عليه: قوله تعالى: (وكنا به عالمين) وذلك لأنه تعالى إنما يخص بالنبوة من يعلم من حاله أنه في المستقبل يقوم بحقها ويجتنب عن كل ما لا يليق بها. ولذلك قال: الله أعلم حيث يجعل رسالته.
سلمنا ذلك. فلم لا يجوز أن يقال: المراد من هذا الرشد، هو ما آتاه الله من العقل والفهم الكامل؟ قوله: العقل حاصل في حق الكل. قلنا: لفظ الرشد إنما يطلق فيمن انتفع بعقله واستعمله في تحصيل مصالحه. والجواب: إن الرشد هو الاهتداء إلى وجوه المصالح. قال تعالى: (فإن أنستم منهم رشداً فادفعوا إليهم أموالهم) ولا يمكن أن يكون المراد التعظيم، ولا يجوز تخصصه بالنبوة. لأن لفظ الرشد مطلق، فتقييده بالنبوة خلاف الظاهر، فوجب حمله على كل ما كان حاصلاً له من العلوم والأفعال الشريفة. وحينئذ يحصل المطلوب.
And We were knowing of him, and the giving of right guidance (rushd) is not an expression of the original intellect and soundness of temperament, because that is present in the majority. So it is not permissible to mention it in the context of favor upon him. Nor is the intended meaning also: success and elucidation, because all that is present in the case of the disbelievers. So it must be that the intended meaning is: what occurred with him of abundant knowledges and noble actions. And that indicates that the actions of the servant occur by the creation of Allah (khalaq Allah).
If it is said: Why is it not permissible that the intended meaning of rushd be prophethood? The evidence for it is His statement: 'And We were knowing of him' — that is because He, exalted, only singles out for prophethood one whom He knows from his state that in the future he will fulfill its due and avoid everything unsuitable for it. Therefore He said: Allah knows best where to place His message.
We grant that. Then why is it not permissible to say that the intended meaning of this rushd is what Allah gave him of intellect and complete understanding? It is said: Intellect is present in everyone. We said: The term rushd is only applied to one who benefits from his intellect and uses it in obtaining his interests. And the answer: Indeed rushd is guidance to the aspects of interests. He said, exalted: 'If you perceive in them right guidance, then give them their wealth.' And it cannot be that the intended meaning is flattery, nor is it permissible to limit it to prophethood, because the term rushd is absolute, so restricting it to prophethood is contrary to the apparent meaning. So it must be interpreted as everything that occurred with him of knowledges and noble actions. And thereby the desired end is attained.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof. It begins by analyzing the term 'rushd' (right guidance) given to Abraham in the Quran. The author argues that 'rushd' cannot refer to basic intellect or physical health, as these are common to most people and would not be a special favor. It also cannot mean general divine guidance or elucidation, as disbelievers also experience that. Therefore, 'rushd' must refer to the abundant knowledge and noble actions that God granted Abraham specifically. This proves that human actions are created by God. The author then addresses two objections. The first objection suggests that 'rushd' means prophethood, citing God's knowledge of Abraham's future piety. The author responds to this implicitly by maintaining the absolute meaning of the word. The second objection suggests that 'rushd' could mean intellect and understanding. The author responds that intellect is universal, but 'rushd' is specifically used for one who uses his intellect beneficially. Finally, the author concludes by quoting the Quranic verse about giving wealth to orphans upon reaching maturity, showing that 'rushd' here means sound judgment, not prophethood or mere intellect. Thus, the term must encompass all the knowledge and noble acts granted to Abraham, supporting the Ash'ari doctrine that actions are created by God.

Key Terms
الرشد
العقل
النبوة
أفعال العبد
خلق الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'rushd' in the story of Abraham is often misunderstood as simply general guidance, intellect, or prophethood.
Correction: The author argues that 'rushd' must be specifically the abundant knowledge and noble actions granted by God to Abraham, and this proves that human actions are created by God.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the correct meaning of 'rushd' given to Abraham?
A) Basic intellect and sound health
B) Prophethood
C) Abundant knowledge and noble actions
D) General divine guidance

Key Takeaway
The special grant of 'rushd' to Abraham encompasses both knowledge and actions, demonstrating that God creates human acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What would it take for you to genuinely attribute every good action to Allah's creation, even when you feel you have worked hard for it?
?
The author argues that even disbelievers receive general success and elucidation from God, but the special 'rushd' of Abraham was unique. What evidence in your life shows that your faith is a special grant from Allah, and not just inherited habit?
?
If human actions are created by Allah, what does that mean for your sense of responsibility? Does this make you more humble and reliant on God, or could it lead to negligence? How can you reconcile the fact that Allah creates your actions with the command to strive and choose righteousness? This lesson demands a response: what will change in how you pray, work, or repent after realizing that even your 'good choices' are ultimately from Allah's creation?

The Eighteenth Quranic Proof: God Creates Evil Acts
Sanusi presents a Quranic proof, objection, and refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the verse about God inciting enmity among Christians proves that God creates evil actions, and how the objection that denouncing evil is always good is refuted.

Core Idea
Denouncing evil is only good if done for the right reason; otherwise, it can itself be evil.

This lesson examines a key Quranic argument from the Ash'ari tradition. The verse about Christians being incited to enmity is used to show that God directly causes evil deeds. The objection tries to reinterpret the verse as describing good behavior, but the answer carefully distinguishes motives for denouncing evil.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and argument
Start by reading the Quranic verse (5:14) and the author's initial claim: God incited enmity, leading to fighting, which are evil deeds. Notice how this directly connects God to creating evil.
The verse ends with 'until the Day of Resurrection,' emphasizing the persistence of enmity.
2
Understand the objection
The objection reinterprets 'incited among them' to mean God incited Christians and Jews against each other's disbelief, which is denouncing evil—a good act. Therefore, the enmity is not evil.
The objector assumes that denouncing evil is always good, which would make God's action good, not evil.
3
Analyze the answer
The author replies by distinguishing intentions: denouncing evil is good only if done because it is evil. If done to lead to another evil (e.g., hatred that causes fighting), it becomes evil. Thus, the enmity remains evil and created by God.
This refutes the Mu'tazilite position that God cannot create evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة عشر: قوله تعالى: (ومن الذين قالوا: إنا نصارى، أخذنا ميثاقهم، فنسوا حظاً مما ذكروا به، فأغرينا بينهم العداوة والبغضاء إلى القيامة). دل هذا النص على أنه تعالى أوقع الإغراء بينهم حتى تقاتلوا. ولا شك أن تلك العداوة والمقاتلة أفعال قبيحة. فإن قالوا: قوله (فأغرينا بينهم) راجع إلى النصارى وإلى من تقدم ذكرهم من اليهود، فالله تعالى أغرى بين النصارى واليهود فيما كان يفعله كل فريق منهم من ضروب الكفر بالله. وإنكار القبيح ليس بقبيح بل هو حسن. فدل ذلك على أن تلك العداوة ليست من القبائح.
والجواب: إن إنكار القبيح إنما يحسن إذا أنكره لكونه قبيحاً. أما إذا أنكره لغرض أن يدعوه إلى قبيح آخر كان قبيحاً. وحينئذ يحصل المطلوب.
The eighteenth proof: His statement, exalted: 'And from those who said, "We are Christians," We took their covenant, but they forgot a portion of that of which they were reminded. So We incited among them enmity and hatred until the Day of Resurrection.' This text indicates that He, exalted, caused incitement among them until they fought each other. And there is no doubt that that enmity and fighting are ugly deeds (af'al qabiha). If they say: His statement 'So We incited among them' refers to the Christians and to those previously mentioned of the Jews, so Allah, exalted, incited between the Christians and the Jews in what each group did of kinds of disbelief in Allah. And denouncing the ugly (inkar al-qabih) is not ugly, rather it is good. So that indicates that that enmity is not among the ugly things.
And the answer: Indeed, denouncing the ugly is only good if one denounces it because it is ugly. But if one denounces it for the purpose of calling to another ugly deed, then it is ugly. And thereby the desired end is attained.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is a proof (hujja) from the Quran followed by an objection and its answer. The author cites Quran 5:14, which states that God incited enmity and hatred among Christians until the Day of Resurrection. The author argues that this incitement led to fighting, which are undoubtedly evil actions (af'al qabiha). Therefore, God directly caused evil actions, supporting the Ash'ari position that God creates all actions, including evil ones. The objection contends that the phrase 'incited among them' refers to both Christians and Jews, and that the enmity between them was a form of denouncing each other's disbelief, which is good, not evil. Thus, the enmity is not truly ugly. The author answers by distinguishing intent: denouncing evil is good if done because it is evil, but if done to lead to another evil (such as hatred leading to greater sins), it becomes evil. In this case, the enmity led to mutual fighting and was not purely for the sake of good, so it remains evil and created by God. This refutes the Mu'tazila who deny that God creates evil acts.

Key Terms
العداوة
البغضاء
إنكار القبيح
القبيح
الإغراء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that denouncing evil is always good regardless of motive.
Correction: The author shows that denouncing evil can be evil if it is done to cause another evil, as in this case where it led to hatred and fighting.

Checkpoint
What is the key distinction in the answer to the objection regarding denouncing evil?
A) Denouncing evil is always good regardless of motive
B) Denouncing evil is good only if done for its own sake, not to cause another evil
C) Denouncing evil is only permissible for prophets
D) Denouncing evil is never good if it leads to conflict

Key Takeaway
God's creation of evil actions is supported by the Quranic claim that He incited enmity, and the objection that denouncing evil is always good is refuted by considering the motive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you denounce an evil in someone else, do you first examine your own heart to ensure you are condemning it because it is evil before God, not because it benefits your ego or agenda? What would it take for you to be completely honest about your motive right now?
?
Think of a specific time you criticized a fellow Muslim. Was your intention truly to uphold truth, or was there a hidden desire to feel superior or to push them away? How can you make amends if your denunciation was tainted?
?
This lesson teaches that even a good act like denouncing evil can become evil if done for the wrong purpose. How will you change your approach to commanding good and forbidding evil starting tomorrow? What concrete step will you take to ensure your intentions are pure?

The Nineteenth Quranic Proof: Two Arguments from Quran 5:64
Presents two arguments from Quran 5:64 for divine creation of actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam ar-Razi uses Quran 5:64 to argue that God creates human actions, specifically through the increase of transgression and disbelief and the casting of enmity.

Core Idea
God's causing the disbelievers to hear revelation can increase their disbelief, proving He creates actions.

This passage presents the nineteenth Quranic proof from Surah al-Ma'idah (5:64). The author extracts two arguments from the verse to support the Ash'ari position that God is the Creator of all actions, including disbelief. The first argument is explained in detail, while the second is briefly referenced as similar to a previous proof. Let's examine how the reasoning unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and identify its two parts
Read the Arabic verse and translation carefully. Note the two phrases: 'increase them in transgression and disbelief' and 'cast among them enmity and hatred.' These form the basis of the two arguments.
The verse is Quran 5:64.
2
Understand the first argument: hearing the verses creates preponderance
The author argues that God's causing the disbelievers to hear the verses creates a preponderance (tarjih) towards disbelief. Since preponderance necessitates the act, God's action entails their disbelief.
This directly counters the Mu'tazila view that humans create their own actions.
3
Reflect on why this contradicts the Mu'tazila
The Mu'tazila deny that God creates evil actions. If God's causing them to hear the verses leads to their disbelief, then God is responsible for that disbelief. This supports the Ash'ari doctrine of divine creation of all actions.
4
Note the second argument and its connection to the previous proof
The second argument from 'casting enmity' is not elaborated here; the author says it is as previously mentioned (the eighteenth proof). Reflect on how it similarly shows God's control over human relations.
Review the previous lesson if needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة عشر: قوله تعالى: (وليزيدن كثيراً منهم ما أنزل إليك من ربك طغياناً وكفراً، وألقينا بينهم العداوة والبغضاء إلى يوم القيامة). والاستدلال به من وجهين:
الأول: إن قوله: (وليزيدن كثيراً منهم ما أنزل إليك من ربك طغياناً وكفراً) معناه: أن إسماع الله إياهم هذه الآيات اقتضى أن يحصل لهم زيادة ميل ورغبة في الكفر. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول: إسماع تلك الآيات اقتضى ترجيح فعل الكفر على فعل الإيمان. وقد دللنا على أن عند حصول الرجحان يجب الفعل. فثبت: أن إسماع الله تعالى إياهم هذه الآيات يستلزم حصول الكفر. وذلك ينافي قول المعتزلة.
والوجه الثاني في الاستدلال: قوله تعالى: (وألقينا بينهم العداوة والبغضاء إلى يوم القيامة). والاستدلال به كما تقدم.
The nineteenth proof: His statement, exalted: 'And what has been revealed to you from your Lord will surely increase many of them in transgression and disbelief. And We have cast among them enmity and hatred until the Day of Resurrection.' And deduction from it is in two aspects:
The first: Indeed, His statement: 'And what has been revealed to you from your Lord will surely increase many of them in transgression and disbelief' means that Allah's causing them to hear these verses necessitated that they obtain an increase in inclination and desire for disbelief. If this is established, then we say: Causing them to hear those verses necessitated a preponderance (tarjih) of the act of disbelief over the act of faith. And we have demonstrated that when preponderance (rujhan) occurs, the act becomes necessary. So it is established that Allah's causing them to hear these verses entails the occurrence of disbelief. And that contradicts the statement of the Mu'tazila.
The second aspect of deduction: His statement, exalted: 'And We have cast among them enmity and hatred until the Day of Resurrection.' And the deduction from it is as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from Quran 5:64 with two distinct arguments. The first argument focuses on the phrase 'increase them in transgression and disbelief.' The author explains that when God causes the disbelievers to hear the verses, it results in an increased inclination towards disbelief. Since a preponderance (tarjih) towards disbelief arises, and the author has previously established that when a preponderance for an action exists, the action becomes necessary, it follows that God's action of making them hear the verses necessitates their disbelief. This directly contradicts the Mu'tazila, who deny that God creates human actions. The second argument is based on the phrase 'We have cast among them enmity and hatred.' This is similar to the previous proof (the eighteenth) and supports the same conclusion that God creates evil actions. The author thus uses two aspects of a single verse to argue for the Ash'ari position on divine creation of actions.

Key Terms
طغيان
كفر
إسماع
ترجيح
رجحان
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Hearing the Quran naturally leads to faith and guidance for everyone.
Correction: The author shows that for some, hearing the Quran increases their disbelief because God creates a preponderance towards it, proving that God controls all actions.

Checkpoint
According to the first aspect of the nineteenth proof, what does God's causing the disbelievers to hear the verses entail?
A) It naturally leads to their faith
B) It increases their disbelief because it creates a preponderance towards it
C) It has no effect on their actions
D) It only affects their knowledge, not their will

Key Takeaway
The increase of transgression and disbelief in the disbelievers when they hear the Quran is evidence that God creates their actions, not that they are independent agents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the Quran, does it increase your faith or your hardness? If Allah can cause the same words to increase disbelief for some, what does that mean for the state of your heart right now?
?
Are you relying on your own efforts to remain steadfast, or have you recognized that even your desire to obey is created by Allah? How does that change your prayer and dependence on Him?
?
The verse warns that Allah can cast enmity between people. Is there any enmity or hatred in your heart that you have not surrendered to Allah? What would it mean to ask Him to remove it, knowing He alone controls hearts?

The Sixteenth Proof: Hadith of Musa and the Samiri
The author cites a hadith dialogue between Moses and God as proof of divine causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a hadith dialogue between Moses and God is used as a proof that God is the ultimate cause of all actions, including misguidance.

Core Idea
In the hadith, God affirms that He alone is the ultimate cause of misguidance, not the Samiri.

This lesson presents the sixteenth proof from the author, which is a powerful hadith. Through a dialogue between Moses and God, the hadith reveals that while the Samiri was the proximate cause of the Israelites' calf worship, God is the ultimate creator of all actions. This supports the Ash'ari position on causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith carefully
The hadith is a narrative with a dialogue. Read it to understand the flow of the conversation between Moses and God.
Pay attention to the sequence of questions Moses asks.
2
Identify the key theological point
Notice that Moses traces the misguidance back to God, and God affirms it. This is the core of the proof.
3
Distinguish proximate vs ultimate cause
The hadith shows the Samiri as proximate cause (making the calf) but God as ultimate cause (putting the spirit). This distinction resolves apparent contradictions.
Consider how this applies to other actions.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this hadith supports the Ash'ari view that God creates all human actions, countering the idea of independent human agency.
Think about the broader implications for divine omnipotence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة عشر : ما رواه الزهري عن عبد الرحمن بن كعب بن أبيه ، قال : قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : ( إن الله تعالى لما وعد موسى ان يكلمه ، خرج في الوقت الذي وعده الله ، قال : « فبينا هو يناجي ربه ، إذ خلفه صوتاً . فقال : اللهم اسمع خلفي صوتاً . لعل قومي ضلوا قال : نعم يا موسى . قال : إلهي . فمن أضلهم ؟ فقال : أضلهم السامري . قال : إلهي . فيم أضلهم ؟ قال : صاغ لهم عجلاً جسداً ، له خوار . قال : إلهي . هذا السامري ، صاغ لهم العجل ، فمن نفخ فيه الروح ، حتى صار له خوار ؟ قال : أنا يا موسى . قال : فوعزتك يا إلهي . ما أضل قومي غيرك قال : صدقت يا حكيم الحكماء . لا ينبغي الحكيم أن يكون أحكم منك
The sixteenth proof: What al-Zuhri narrated from 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Ka'b ibn Abih, who said: The Messenger of God, may God bless him and grant him peace, said: 'When God Almighty promised Moses to speak to him, he went out at the time God had promised him. He said: While he was conversing with his Lord, suddenly behind him a voice. So he said: O God, I hear a voice behind me. Perhaps my people have gone astray. He said: Yes, O Moses. He said: My God, who led them astray? He said: The Samiri led them astray. He said: My God, in what did he lead them astray? He said: He fashioned for them a calf, a body, with a lowing sound. He said: My God, this Samiri fashioned the calf for them, so who breathed the spirit into it until it had a lowing sound? He said: I, O Moses. He said: Then by Your might, my God, none led my people astray except You. He said: You have spoken the truth, O wisest of the wise. It is not fitting that the wise should be wiser than you.'
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage presents the sixteenth proof, which is a hadith narrated by al-Zuhri. The hadith describes a dialogue between Moses and God after the Israelites worshipped the calf. Moses hears a noise behind him and fears his people have gone astray. God confirms that the Samiri led them astray. Moses then asks about the details: the Samiri made the calf, but who put the spirit in it so that it made a sound? God answers that He did. Moses then swears by God's might that none led his people astray except God, and God affirms this, calling Moses the wisest of the wise. This hadith is used by the author to support the Ash'ari position that God is the sole creator of all actions, including misguidance, while the Samiri is only a proximate cause.

السامري
إضلال
سبب قريب
سبب بعيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith contradicts the Quran because it says God led them astray, not the Samiri.
Correction: The hadith harmonizes with the Quran by distinguishing proximate cause (Samiri) and ultimate cause (God).

Checkpoint
In the hadith, who does Moses ultimately attribute the misguidance of his people to?
A) The Samiri
B) Himself
C) God
D) The calf

Key Takeaway
The hadith of Musa and the Samiri demonstrates that while secondary causes exist, God is the ultimate creator of all actions, including misguidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face trials or failures, do you truly trace the ultimate cause back to God, or do you stop at the visible secondary causes? How can you train yourself to see His hand behind every event?
?
Moses boldly affirmed that none led his people astray except God. Are you willing to accept that your own straying from the straight path, when it occurs, is ultimately from God? How does that realization affect your repentance and reliance on Him?
?
If you were in Moses' position, would you have dared to say 'none led them astray except You'? What holds you back from acknowledging God's absolute sovereignty over all actions, including your own?

Resolving Apparent Contradictions: Proximate vs Remote Cause
Al-Ka'bi's first two objections and author's response using proximate/remote cause distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author resolves the apparent contradiction between the hadith and the Quran by distinguishing between proximate and remote causation.

Core Idea
The same action can be attributed to both a proximate cause (creature) and a remote cause (God) without contradiction.

Al-Ka'bi objects that the hadith makes Moses appear to contradict the Quran by attributing misguidance to God. The author answers by introducing a key distinction in causality: the Samiri is the proximate cause (direct agent), while God is the remote cause (creator of power and motive). This dissolution of the contradiction also answers the second objection about Moses being wiser than God.

Guided Path
1
Read Al-Ka'bi's first two objections
Identify the two objections: (1) the hadith implies Moses accused God of lying, (2) it makes Moses seem more knowledgeable than God. Notice how they rely on a literal reading of attributions.
The objections assume that if God attributes misguidance to the Samiri, then attributing it to God would be contradictory.
2
Study the author's response
Read the response carefully. The author explains that the Samiri is the proximate cause (his action directly led to their straying) and God is the remote cause (He created the power and motive). Thus both attributions are valid and no contradiction exists.
The response uses the principle 'the combination of power and motive necessitates the act.'
3
See how the second objection is also answered
The author shows that attributing to God as remote cause does not make Moses wiser than God; it simply recognizes God as the ultimate cause of causes.
The distinction between proximate and remote cause refutes the claim of superior wisdom.
4
Reflect on the doctrinal significance
Consider how this distinction preserves both divine causality and human agency, a key theme in Sunni kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الكعبي في بعض تصانيفه : هذا الخبر ضعيف ، وبيانه من وجوه : الأول : إن هذا الخبر ، اشتمل على تكذيب الله . لأنه تعالى قال : وأضلهم السامري (۱) ) والحديث دل على أن موسى حلف بعزة الله : أنه ما اضلهم غير الله . وإذا كان المضل هو الله ، فالسامري لا يكون مضلا لهم ، مع أن الله تعالى قال : ( وأضلهم السامري ) فيكون هذا تصريحاً من موسى بتكذيب الله ، وحاشاه من مثل هذا المقال . والثاني : في هذا الحديث. أن موسى أحكم الحكماء .. بسبب أنه أضاف ذلك الإضلال إلى الله ، وأن الله أضاف الإضلال إلى السامري في القرآن ، حيث قال : ( وأضلهم السامري ) وهذا يقتضي أن يكون موسى عليه السلام أكثر حكمة وعلماً من الله - تعالى عما يقول الظالمون - . والثالث : إن موسى عند اشتغاله بمناجاة الله ، ما كان بحيث يسمع صوت قومه . والرابع : إنه لما سمع ذلك الصوت ، لم يفهم كلامهم ، فكيف عرف بمجرد سماع الصوت ، أن القوم قد ضلوا ؟ . والجواب عن الأول : ظاهر على قولنا : إن مجموع القدرة مع الداعي ، يوجب الفعل . وذلك لأن السامري ( أضلهم على معنى أنهم إنما ضلوا بسبب الفعل الصادر عنه . والله تعالى أضلهم بمعنى أنه تعالى هو الذي خلق القدرة والداعية ، اللذين صار مجموعهما موجبا لحصول ذلك الفعل . فأحدهما : سبب قريب . والآخر : سبب بعيد . و به خرج الجواب عن السؤال الثاني : وذلك لأنه تعالى أضاف الإضلال إلى السامري ، إضافة الحكم إلى السبب القريب. وموسى له أضافه إلى الله تعالى إضافة الحكم إلى السبب البعيد . والمبدأ الأول لما بينـه مـوسـى عليـه السلام ، أنه المؤثر الأول ، الذي هو سبب الأسباب ، لا جرم قال تعالى : إنك أحكم الحكماء ) ولا يلزم من هذا القدر أن يكون أكثر حكمة من الله . وأما قوله ثالثاً : ( كيف سمع موسى هناك أصوات القوم ؟ فنقول : ذلك الزمان أمكن انخراق العادات. وعندنا لا يبعد أن يخلق الله سمعاً للإنسان يسمع به الأصوات المتباعدة جداً . وأما قوله رابعاً : ( هب أنه سمع الأصوات ، فكيف عرف أنهم
Al-Ka'bi said in some of his writings: This report is weak, and its explanation is from several aspects: First: This report includes disbelieving God, because God Almighty said: 'And the Samiri led them astray' (Quran 20:85). The hadith indicates that Moses swore by God's might that none led them astray but God. And if the one who leads astray is God, then the Samiri would not be leading them astray, while God Almighty said: 'And the Samiri led them astray.' Thus this would be an explicit statement from Moses disbelieving God, far be it from him from such a statement. Second: In this hadith, Moses is the wisest of the wise because he attributed that misguidance to God, while God attributed misguidance to the Samiri in the Quran, where He said: 'And the Samiri led them astray.' And this requires that Moses, peace be upon him, be wiser and more knowledgeable than God - exalted is He above what the wrongdoers say. Third: Moses, while occupied with his private conversation with God, was not in a position to hear the voice of his people. Fourth: When he heard that voice, he did not understand their speech, so how did he know from merely hearing the voice that the people had gone astray? The answer to the first: It is clear according to our view: that the combination of power (qudra) with the motive (da'i) necessitates the act. That is because the Samiri led them astray in the sense that they only went astray due to the act emanating from him. And God Almighty led them astray in the sense that He is the one who created the power and the motive, the combination of which necessitated the occurrence of that act. So one of them is a proximate cause, and the other is a remote cause. And by this emerges the answer to the second question: That is because God attributed misguidance to the Samiri as an attribution of the judgment to the proximate cause, while Moses attributed it to God Almighty as an attribution of the judgment to the remote cause. And the first principle, as Moses, peace be upon him, made clear, is that He is the first agent, who is the cause of causes. Therefore He said: 'You are the wisest of the wise,' and it does not follow from this that he is wiser than God. As for his third statement: 'How did Moses hear the voices of the people there?' We say: At that time, the breaking of customs was possible. And according to us, it is not improbable that God creates hearing for a person by which he hears very distant sounds. As for his fourth statement: 'Granted that he heard the voices, how did he know that they...'
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage presents al-Ka'bi's objections to the hadith and the author's responses. First, al-Ka'bi claims the hadith implies Moses disbelieved God because God says 'the Samiri led them astray' while Moses says 'none led them astray but You.' Second, the hadith makes Moses wiser than God by attributing misguidance to God while God attributes it to the Samiri. Third, Moses was engaged in prayer and could not hear his people. Fourth, even if he heard, he could not know they had gone astray without understanding their speech. The author responds by distinguishing between proximate and remote causes: the Samiri is the proximate cause (because his action directly led to their straying), while God is the remote cause (because He created the power and motive that necessitated the action). Thus, both attributions are correct and there is no contradiction. The second objection is answered similarly: attributing to the proximate cause (Samiri) and to the remote cause (God) does not make Moses wiser; it simply recognizes that God is the first cause of all causes. Regarding the third objection, the author argues that God can break natural customs and create hearing for distant sounds. The response to the fourth objection is incomplete in this passage.

Key Terms
الكعبي
سبب قريب
سبب بعيد
قدرة
داعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the hadith and Quran contradict each other regarding who led the people astray.
Correction: The author shows that they are compatible: the Samiri is the proximate cause, God is the remote cause of the misguidance.

Checkpoint
How does the author resolve the apparent contradiction between the hadith (Moses attributing misguidance to God) and the Quran (God attributing misguidance to the Samiri)?
A) By rejecting the hadith as weak
B) By arguing that the Samiri's act was not real misguidance
C) By distinguishing between proximate cause (Samiri) and remote cause (God)
D) By claiming Moses was not actually attributing misguidance to God

Key Takeaway
The distinction between proximate and remote cause harmonizes apparent contradictions between divine and creatural causation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When someone harms you, do you stop at resentment toward the immediate cause, or do you actively seek to see God's permission and wisdom behind that person's action? How might that shift your emotional response?
?
If you truly believe that every impulse and ability to act comes from God, how does that affect your sense of responsibility for your own sins? Does it lead you to deeper humility in repentance or to complacency? Reflect honestly.
?
Recall a mistake you made where you blamed yourself entirely. Now consider that God created the power and motive in that moment. Does that realization increase your trust in God's mercy and your need for His guidance, or does it unsettle you? What will you do with that realization today?

Moses Hearing the People: The Breaking of Natural Customs
Al-Ka'bi's third objection and author's response using the concept of breaking of customs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the hadith by appealing to the possibility of God breaking natural customs (kharq al-‘adat).

Core Idea
God can break natural laws to enable miracles, such as allowing Moses to hear his people from afar.

Al-Ka'bi's third objection questions how Moses could hear the voices of his people while engaged in private conversation with God. The author responds by affirming that God can suspend natural laws, creating extraordinary hearing. This lesson examines that objection and the principle of kharq al-‘adat (breaking of customs), a key concept in Sunni theology for defending miracles.

Guided Path
1
Read the third objection
Al-Ka'bi argues that Moses could not have heard his people while engrossed in prayer. Identify the perceived impossibility.
The objection relies on normal physical limitations.
2
Examine the author's response
The author invokes the concept of 'breaking of customs' (kharq al-‘adat), asserting that God can enable hearing over extraordinary distances.
This is a standard argument for miracles in Ash'ari theology.
3
Connect to broader theology
Reflect on how this response upholds divine omnipotence while accepting normal causal patterns.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that miracles are impossible because they violate natural laws.
Correction: In Ash'ari theology, natural laws are merely God's customs; God can break them at any time, so miracles like Moses hearing from afar are possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God can intervene in your life in ways that defy all natural expectations, or do you unconsciously limit Him by what you consider possible?
?
When you face an impossible situation, does your heart immediately turn to the One who breaks customs for His prophets, or do you rely solely on natural means?
?
In what specific area of your life have you been placing your trust in the laws of cause and effect rather than in the Creator who can suspend them at any moment?

Proximate and Remote Causes in Divine Action
Objections 1–2 and their answer on causal attribution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari theology resolves apparent contradictions by distinguishing between proximate and remote causes.

Core Idea
Attributing an act to both God and a creature is consistent — they act at different causal levels.

In this passage, the author addresses two objections that arise from Moses' statement about God leading the people astray, while the Quran attributes misguidance to al-Samiri. The answer introduces a key Ash'ari principle: the combination of power and motive necessitates action, and causality operates at two levels — proximate and remote. This clarifies why both attributions are valid.

Guided Path
1
Read the two objections
Carefully read the first and second objections. The first claims Moses would be contradicting God; the second claims Moses would be wiser than God.
These objections arise from a literal reading of the texts.
2
Identify the causal principle
In the answer, note the principle: the combination of power (qudra) and motive (dā'ī) necessitates the action. This is a foundational Ash'ari concept.
This principle allows for multiple causal attributions.
3
Understand proximate vs remote cause
The answer distinguishes between al-Samiri as proximate cause (sabab qarīb) and God as remote cause (sabab ba'īd). See how this resolves both objections.
This distinction is key to Ash'ari occasionalism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن الله تعالى قال : ( وأضلهم السامري ) فيكون هذا تصريحاً من موسى بتكذيب الله ، وحاشاه من مثل هذا المقال . والثاني : في هذا الحديث. أن موسى أحكم الحكماء .. بسبب أنه أضاف ذلك الإضلال إلى الله ، وأن الله أضاف الإضلال إلى السامري في القرآن ، حيث قال : ( وأضلهم السامري ) وهذا يقتضي أن يكون موسى عليه السلام أكثر حكمة وعلماً من الله - تعالى عما يقول الظالمون - . والثالث : إن موسى عند اشتغاله بمناجاة الله ، ما كان بحيث يسمع صوت قومه . والرابع : إنه لما سمع ذلك الصوت ، لم يفهم كلامهم ، فكيف عرف بمجرد سماع الصوت ، أن القوم قد ضلوا ؟ والجواب عن الأول : ظاهر على قولنا : إن مجموع القدرة مع الداعي ، يوجب الفعل . وذلك لأن السامري ( أضلهم على معنى أنهم إنما ضلوا بسبب الفعل الصادر عنه . والله تعالى أضلهم بمعنى أنه تعالى هو الذي خلق القدرة والداعية ، اللذين صار مجموعهما موجبا لحصول ذلك الفعل . فأحدهما : سبب قريب . والآخر : سبب بعيد . و به خرج الجواب عن السؤال الثاني : وذلك لأنه تعالى أضاف الإضلال إلى السامري ، إضافة الحكم إلى السبب القريب. وموسى له أضافه إلى الله تعالى إضافة الحكم إلى السبب البعيد . والمبدأ الأول لما بينه موسى عليه السلام ، أنه المؤثر الأول ، الذي هو سبب الأسباب ، لا جرم قال تعالى : إنك أحكم الحكماء ) ولا يلزم من هذا القدر أن يكون أكثر حكمة من الله . وأما قوله ثالثاً : ( كيف سمع موسى هناك أصوات القوم ؟ فنقول : ذلك الزمان أمكن انخراق العادات. وعندنا لا يبعد أن يخلق الله سمعاً للإنسان يسمع به الأصوات المتباعدة جداً . وأما قوله رابعاً : ( هب أنه سمع الأصوات ، فكيف عرف أنهم ضلوا ؟ قلنا : بالطريق الذي ثبت جواز أن سمع به تلك الأصوات البعيدة ، ثبت أيضاً جواز أن يفهم كلامهم . صريح وبهذا الطريق سقطت هذه الكلمات . والله أعلم
That Allah Almighty said: 'And al-Samiri led them astray' so this would be an explicit statement from Moses of disbelieving in Allah, far be he from such a saying. The second: in this hadith, that Moses is the wisest of the wise... because he attributed that misguidance to Allah, while Allah attributed misguidance to al-Samiri in the Qur'an, where He said: 'And al-Samiri led them astray.' And this requires that Moses, peace be upon him, be wiser and more knowledgeable than Allah - exalted is Allah above what the wrongdoers say. The third: that Moses, while occupied in intimate conversation with Allah, was not such that he could hear the voice of his people. The fourth: that when he heard that voice, he did not understand their speech, so how did he know by merely hearing the voice that the people had gone astray? The answer to the first: it is clear according to our statement that the combination of power with the motive necessitates the action. That is because al-Samiri led them astray in the sense that they only went astray due to the action emanating from him. And Allah Almighty led them astray in the sense that He is the One who created the power and the motive, the combination of which necessitated the occurrence of that action. So one of them is a proximate cause, and the other is a remote cause. And by this emerges the answer to the second question: that is because He attributed misguidance to al-Samiri, attributing the judgment to the proximate cause, while Moses attributed it to Allah Almighty, attributing the judgment to the remote cause. And the First Principle, which Moses, peace be upon him, made clear, is that He is the first effective cause, who is the cause of causes, so He said: 'Indeed, you are the wisest of the wise.' And it does not necessarily follow from this that he is wiser than Allah. As for his third statement: 'How did Moses hear the voices of the people there?' We say: At that time, the breaking of customs was possible. And according to us, it is not far-fetched that Allah should create for a human a hearing by which he hears very distant sounds. As for his fourth statement: 'Grant that he heard the sounds, how did he know that they had gone astray?' We say: By the same way that established the permissibility of hearing those distant sounds, it also established the permissibility of understanding their speech. Explicitly, and by this way these words are refuted. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage presents four objections against a view that attributes misguidance to both God and al-Samiri, followed by answers. The first objection claims that if Moses said 'God led them astray' while God said 'al-Samiri led them astray,' then Moses would be contradicting God, which is impossible for a prophet. The second objection argues that Moses would be wiser than God because he attributed misguidance to God, while God attributed it to a creature. The third objection questions how Moses could hear his people's voices while engaged in divine communion. The fourth objection asks how Moses could know they were astray just by hearing voices without understanding words. 

The author's answer to the first and second objections is based on the Ash'ari principle that the combination of power (qudra) and motive (dā'ī) necessitates the action. Al-Samiri is the proximate cause (sabab qarīb) of their misguidance because the action emanated from him, while God is the remote cause (sabab ba'īd) as He created the power and motive that together produced the act. Thus attributing misguidance to al-Samiri as proximate cause and to God as ultimate cause is consistent, and Moses' attribution to God as the First Principle does not imply superiority over God. The answer to the third objection appeals to the possibility of miracles (kharq al-‘ādāt): God could have created in Moses the ability to hear distant sounds. The answer to the fourth objection extends this: the same divine power that enabled hearing could enable understanding. Thus all objections are refuted.

Key Terms
سبب قريب
سبب بعيد
مجموع القدرة مع الداعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Attributing an act to both God and a creature is contradictory or impossible.
Correction: The Ash'ari distinction between proximate and remote causes shows that both attributions are valid and complementary.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what role does al-Samiri play in the misguidance?
A) Ultimate cause
B) Proximate cause
C) No causal role
D) The one who created power and motive

Key Takeaway
The same action can be attributed to God as remote cause and to a creature as proximate cause without contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you primarily blame your own weakness or secretly feel that God decreed it and therefore you are not fully responsible? How does this lesson's distinction between proximate and remote cause call you to repent sincerely and take ownership of your actions?
?
Moses, the prophet, attributed misguidance to God while acknowledging al-Samiri as the immediate agent. Are you willing to credit God for your successes without denying your own effort, and accept your failures as ultimately from your own soul? What specific step will you take today to align your heart with this balanced attribution?
?
This lesson teaches that the same act can be attributed to God and a creature at different levels. Does your heart truly rest in knowing that God is the cause behind all causes, even the painful ones? Or do you resent Him for certain outcomes? Will you now submit to His decree and work to become a proximate cause of good?

Miracles: Hearing and Understanding Beyond Natural Means
Objections 3–4 and their answer on prophetic miracles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari concept of 'kharq al-‘ādāt' (breaking of customs) explains miraculous abilities of prophets.

Core Idea
God can break natural customs to grant prophets extraordinary perception.

The third and fourth objections question how Moses could hear his people's voices during divine communion and how he understood they were astray. The answer appeals to the possibility of miracles: God can create hearing for distant sounds and understanding of speech beyond normal means. This affirms prophetic miracles and the Ash'ari principle that God is not bound by natural laws.

Guided Path
1
Read the third and fourth objections
The third objection doubts Moses could hear his people while busy with God. The fourth asks how he knew they were astray just by hearing voices.
These objections assume normal human limitations.
2
Understand 'kharq al-‘ādāt'
The answer invokes the possibility of miracles—God can break His usual patterns (customs) and create extraordinary abilities in prophets.
This is a core Ash'ari doctrine: miracles are not violations of law but suspensions of custom.
3
See how the same power answers both
The same divine power that enabled hearing also enabled understanding. The passage argues that if God can create hearing for distant sounds, He can also create understanding without normal speech recognition.
This is a consistent application of the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Preponderant Motive and Action Necessity
The author addresses the objection that a preponderant motive does not always necessitate action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's response to the objection that a preponderant motive does not always necessitate action, and the distinction between opposed and unopposed inclination.

Core Idea
Only an unopposed preponderant inclination necessarily leads to action.

This passage tackles a common objection to the determinist position that a preponderant motive (dā'ī) always leads to action. The objector points to everyday experience where we feel an inclination but do not act. The author responds by refining the concept of preponderance: an inclination that is opposed by a stronger motive is no longer truly preponderant. Only when it is unopposed does it necessitate action.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objector's claim that a preponderant motive does not always make action obligatory, noting the example of inclination without action.
The objector appeals to common experience.
2
Study the author's answer
Read the answer carefully. The author distinguishes between an inclination that is opposed and one that is free from opposition.
Focus on the phrase 'خلى عن المعارض' (free from opposition).
3
Analyze the logical distinction
Consider how opposition nullifies preponderance. When a stronger motive opposes, the initial inclination loses its status as preponderant.
4
Reflect on the implication
Understand that the author's view remains consistent: a truly preponderant and unopposed motive necessarily leads to action, preserving determinism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالوا: ليس كل ما رجح الداعي، فإنه واجب الفعل، فإنه قد يميل قلبنا إلى بعض الأفعال، مع أنا لا نفعل. والجواب: إن ذلك الميل إنما لا يوجب الفعل، لأنه لما صار معارضاً بصادر آخر أقوى منه، فحينئذ لم يبق ميلا. بل الميل المرجح إذا حصل وخلى عن المعارض، فإنه كما يرجح، فلا بد وأن يوجب الفعل كما قررناه.
And they said: 'Not every motive that is preponderant makes the action obligatory, for our heart may incline to some actions, yet we do not act.' The answer: That inclination only does not necessitate action because when it becomes opposed by another stronger source, then it is no longer an inclination. Rather, the preponderant inclination when it occurs and is free from opposition, just as it preponderates, it necessarily necessitates the action as we have established.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the relationship between motive (dā'ī) and action. The objector claims that a preponderant motive does not always lead to action, giving the example of inclination without action. The author responds by distinguishing between an inclination that is opposed by a stronger motive and one that is unopposed. When an inclination is opposed, it loses its preponderance and does not necessitate action. But when it is truly preponderant and unchallenged, it necessarily results in action. This preserves the determinist position that actions are necessitated by the preponderant motive, consistent with the author's earlier arguments.

Key Terms
الداعي (al-dā'ī)
المرجح (al-murajjah)
المعارض (al-mu'āriḍ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any inclination, even if opposed, should lead to action because it is felt strongly.
Correction: The author clarifies that an opposed inclination is not truly preponderant; only an unopposed one necessitates action.

Checkpoint
According to the author, when does a preponderant inclination necessitate action?
A) Always, regardless of opposition
B) Only when it is free from any opposing motive
C) Only when it is weak
D) Never, because free will overrides

Key Takeaway
A preponderant motive only necessitates action when it is unopposed by any stronger motive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel a strong desire to do good but hold back, what is the 'stronger motive' opposing it—fear of people, comfort, or something else? Are you willing to expose and remove that opposition so your true intention can lead to action?
?
Probe your heart: have you ever claimed a desire was sincere when in reality you allowed an opposing motive to dominate, excusing inaction? Can you identify one specific instance where you must now act despite inner resistance?
?
If your eternal destiny is determined by the preponderance of your motives, what is the state of your deepest inclinations right now? Are they truly unopposed in their pursuit of Allah, or are they being overridden by worldly concerns—and what will you change today to tip the scale?
