The Claim and the Motive-Based Proof
First proof: motive-dependence argument for necessary agency knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu al-Husayn's claim that humans have necessary knowledge of their agency and his first proof based on the dependence of actions on motives.

Core Idea
Human actions depend on motives, and this is known necessarily by all rational people.

Abu al-Husayn al-Basri defends the view that every rational person necessarily knows that they are the agents of their own actions. He begins by stating the claim and then presents the first of three proofs, which examines how our actions are always linked to our intentions or motives. In this lesson, we will read the beginning of this argument and see how he establishes the necessary nature of this knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the claim and first proof
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the translation. The passage presents Abu al-Husayn's thesis and his first argument based on motives (dawaʿi).
Notice that the argument starts by stating that every rational person knows from himself that actions depend on motives.
2
Identify the structure of the argument
The first proof has a clear logical flow: premise (actions depend on motives), examples (hunger, poison, child's behavior), definition of 'agent' (mujid), and conclusion (knowledge is necessary). Trace these steps.
The examples are meant to show that this knowledge is intuitive and universal.
3
Reflect on necessary knowledge
Ask yourself: Why does Abu al-Husayn claim this knowledge is 'necessary' (daruri)? What makes it different from inferential knowledge? The commentary will help clarify.
Necessary knowledge is immediate and indubitable, not derived from reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حكاية قيل من يقول: العلم الضروري حاصل بأن العبد موجد. قد نقلنا في أول المسألة: أن أبا الحسين يدعي العلم الضروري بأن العبد موجد الأفعال نفسه. واحتجوا على أن العلم الضروري حاصل بذلك بوجوه ثلاثة:

الأول: إن كل عاقل يعلم من نفسه: أن وقوع تصرفاته موقوف على دواعيه، ولولا دواعيه لما وقع شيء منها. فمتى اشتد به الجوع، وكان تناول الطعام ممكناً، فإنه يصدر منه تناول الطعام. ومتى اعتقد أن في الطعام سما، ينصرف عنه. وكذلك يعلم من غيره من العقلاء، الذي أحوالهم سليمة: أنه يجب وقوع أفعالهم بحسب دواعيهم. فإن الولد العاقل السليم إذا علم ما له من المنفعة في شرب الماء، حال شدة عطشه، ولم يمنعه منه مانع، فإنه يصدر منه الشرب، لا محالة. ومتى علم ما له من المضرة في دخول النار، فإنه لا يدخل فيها. وإذا كان معنى الموجد للشيء: هو الذي يحدث منه الفعل، موافقاً لدواعيه، ثبت أن العقلاء قد يعرفون ببدائه عقولهم كون العبد كذلك. علمنا: أن علمهم بكون العبيد محدثين لتصرفاتهم: علم ضروري.

الوجه الثاني في تقرير ذلك: أن العقلاء يعلمون بالضرورة: حسن ذم من أساء إليهم، ومدح المحسن إليهم. ويعلمون بالضرورة: قبح المدح والذم على كونه طويلاً أو قصيراً، وكون السماء فوقه والأرض تحته. ولولا علمهم الضروري بكون العبد محدثاً لأفعاله، لما علموا بالضرورة حسن مدحه وذمه على أفعاله، وقبح مدحه وذمه على ما لا يكون واقعاً به. لأن العلم بحسن المدح والذم لما كان فرعاً على العلم بحدوث ذلك منهم ـ والعلم بالفرع إذا كان ضرورياً، كان العلم بالأصل أولى أن يكون ضروريا - لزم القطع بأن علم العقلاء بكونهم محدثين لتصرفاتهم: علم ضروري بديهي. ولأجل ظهور هذا العلم عند العقلاء، حصل هذا العلم للمراهقين. فإن الإنسان إذا رمى المراهق بأجرة، فإنه يلم الرامي ولا يذم تلك الأجرة. ولولا أنه عالم بالضرورة: بأن الرامي فاعل، وأن الأجرة ما فعلت شيئاً، وإلا لما فرق بينهما من الوجه المذكور.

الثالث: إن الواحد منا يجد من نفسه - ومن غيره ـ إذا طلب فعلا من غیره، أنه يطلب منه طلب عالم بأنه هو الذي يحدث ذلك الفعل. ولذلك فإنه يتلطف في استدعائه ذلك الفعل منه، بكل لطف وينهاه عن فعل ما يكرهه، ويعنفه على فعله، ويتعجب من فعله، ويستظرفه، ويعجب العقلاء في فعله. ويعلل كل ذلك: بأن فعله. وأيضاً: فنجد من أنفسنا: الفرق الضروري بين أمره بالقيام والقعود، وبين أمره بإيجاد السماء والكواكب. ولولا أن العلم الضروري حاصل بكوننا موجدين أفعالنا، وإلا لما صح ذلك.

قالوا: فهذه الوجوه الثلاثة: منبهة على أن العلم الضروري حاصل يكون العبد موجداً. وهذا غاية تقرير مذهب أبي الحسين البصري، في هذه المسألة.
Account of what is said: The necessary knowledge is obtained that the servant is the agent. We have reported at the beginning of the issue: that Abu al-Husayn claims the necessary knowledge that the servant is the creator of the actions himself. And they argued that the necessary knowledge is obtained thereby by three proofs:

First: Every rational person knows from himself that the occurrence of his actions depends on his motives, and without his motives none of them would occur. So when hunger intensifies for him, and eating food is possible, then eating food issues from him. And when he believes that there is poison in the food, he turns away from it. Similarly, he knows from other rational persons whose states are sound that the occurrence of their actions must be according to their motives. For a rational, sound child, if he knows the benefit in drinking water when his thirst is severe, and no preventer prevents him, then drinking issues from him, inevitably. And if he knows the harm in entering fire, he does not enter it. And if the meaning of 'agent of a thing' is: he from whom the action occurs, in accordance with his motives, it is established that rational persons know by the first principles of their intellects that the servant is such. We know that their knowledge that servants are originators of their actions is necessary knowledge.

Second proof in establishing that: Rational persons know necessarily the goodness of blaming one who does evil to them, and praising the one who does good to them. And they know necessarily the ugliness of praise and blame for being tall or short, and for the sky being above them and the earth beneath them. And if they did not have necessary knowledge that the servant is the originator of his actions, they would not know necessarily the goodness of praising and blaming him for his actions, and the ugliness of praising and blaming him for what does not occur from him. Because knowledge of the goodness of praise and blame, since it is a branch of the knowledge of the occurrence of that from them—and knowledge of the branch, if it is necessary, then knowledge of the root is more worthy of being necessary—it necessarily follows that the knowledge of rational persons that they are originators of their actions is necessary and intuitive. And because of the manifestation of this knowledge among rational persons, this knowledge is obtained by adolescents. For if a person throws a stone at an adolescent, he blames the thrower and does not blame that stone. And if he were not aware necessarily that the thrower is an agent and that the stone did nothing, he would not differentiate between them in the mentioned respect.

Third: Each one of us finds from himself—and from others—when he requests an action from another, that he requests it from him as one who knows that he is the one who brings about that action. Therefore, he is gentle in soliciting that action from him, in every gentleness, and forbids him from what he dislikes, and chides him for his action, and wonders at his action, and considers it elegant, and rational persons marvel at his action. And he explains all that: by it being his action. Also: we find from ourselves the necessary distinction between commanding him to stand and sit, and between commanding him to create the sky and the planets. And if the necessary knowledge that we are agents of our actions were not obtained, that would not be correct.

They said: So these three proofs indicate that the necessary knowledge is obtained that the servant is the agent. And this is the ultimate presentation of the doctrine of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, in this issue.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage presents three proofs advanced by Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and his followers to establish that human beings have necessary (daruri) knowledge that they are the agents (mujidun) of their own actions. The author begins by stating the claim: necessary knowledge exists that the servant (al-ʿabd) is the agent. He then reports that Abu al-Husayn claims this knowledge, and they argue for it with three proofs.

The first proof relies on the human experience of acting according to motives (dawaʿi). Every rational person knows from himself that his actions depend on his motives: when hungry, he eats; when he believes food is poisoned, he refrains. Similarly, he observes this in other rational persons. Since the meaning of 'agent' (mujid) is one from whom an action proceeds in accordance with motives, rational people know by the first principles of their intellects that the servant is such. Therefore, their knowledge that servants are originators of their actions is necessary.

The second proof focuses on the universal human practice of praising and blaming. Rational people necessarily know that it is good to blame one who harms them and praise one who benefits them. They also necessarily know that it is ugly to praise or blame someone for involuntary traits like height or the position of the sky and earth. If they did not have necessary knowledge that the servant originates his actions, they would not make this distinction. Since knowledge of the goodness of praise and blame (a branch) is necessary, the knowledge that humans are agents (the root) must be even more necessary. This is illustrated by the example of a person who throws a stone at an adolescent: the adolescent blames the thrower, not the stone, showing he necessarily knows the thrower is the agent.

The third proof appeals to the way people interact when requesting actions. When someone asks another to do something, he does so as one who knows that the other is the one who will bring about that action. He uses gentle persuasion, forbids disliked actions, chides, wonders, and praises—all based on the assumption that the action belongs to the other. Moreover, people intuitively distinguish between commanding someone to stand or sit (which he can do) and commanding him to create the sky and planets (which he cannot). This distinction would not exist without the necessary knowledge that we are agents of our actions.

In conclusion, the three proofs together indicate that the necessary knowledge that the servant is the agent is indeed obtained, and this is the final presentation of Abu al-Husayn's doctrine on this issue.

Key Terms
علم ضروري
موجد
دواعي
بديهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that these proofs are meant to generate new knowledge about human agency.
Correction: Abu al-Husayn's proofs are not intended to create new knowledge but to highlight what is already known necessarily and self-evidently to all rational people.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, what does every rational person know from himself about his actions?
A) That they are created by God
B) That they depend on his motives
C) That they are predetermined
D) That they are random

Key Takeaway
The first proof establishes that the dependence of actions on motives is known necessarily by all rational people, confirming their agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim that your actions depend on your motives. When you sin, are you honest about the motive that drove you? What would change if you examined every motive before acting, knowing that Allah sees them all?
?
This proof says every rational person necessarily knows they are the agent of their actions. Do you live as if that is true, or do you sometimes excuse your failures by blaming circumstances or others? How would your daily choices change if you fully owned that agency?
?
The child refrains from fire because he knows it harms him. Where is that same clarity in your spiritual life? Are there 'fires' you approach despite knowing the harm, because your motives are disordered? Will you ask Allah tonight to purify your motives?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate

The Second Proof: Praise, Blame, and Necessary Distinction
Second proof: argument from praise/blame and the branch-root analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu al-Husayn's second proof for necessary agency knowledge, based on the universal human practices of praising and blaming.

Core Idea
Praising and blaming only make sense if humans are agents of their actions, and this distinction is known necessarily.

The second proof shifts focus from internal motives to social practices. Abu al-Husayn argues that rational people necessarily know that it is good to blame those who harm them and praise those who help them, while they also know it is absurd to praise or blame someone for involuntary traits. This distinction between actions and non-actions reveals that humans are agents, and this knowledge is necessary. In this lesson, we will explore this argument and its illustration with the stone-throwing example.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof
Read the Arabic and translation of the second proof. Look for the key terms: 'ḥusn dhamm' (goodness of blame), 'qubḥ madḥ' (ugliness of praise), and the branch-root analogy.
The proof is structured as a conditional argument: if praise/blame are known necessarily, then agency must be known even more necessarily.
2
Analyze the branch-root reasoning
The argument claims that knowledge of the goodness of praise and blame (branch) is dependent on knowledge that the person is the agent (root). Since the branch knowledge is necessary, the root must also be necessary.
This is a logical argument from a stronger premise (branch is necessary) to the necessity of the root.
3
Examine the adolescent example
The example of a person throwing a stone at an adolescent shows that the adolescent blames the thrower, not the stone. This demonstrates that even adolescents have necessary knowledge of agency.
The stone is not blamed because it is not an agent; the thrower is blamed because he is the agent.

What does the example of the adolescent and the stone illustrate?
A) That stones are not agents

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you blame someone for a mistake, do you genuinely believe they were the agent of that action, or do you sometimes blame without thinking? How does this lesson challenge your everyday judgments?
?

?
If you truly believed that all actions are created by God without human agency, would your practice of praising and blaming change? Why or why not?

The Third Proof: Requesting Actions and Natural Distinctions
Third proof: argument from request behavior and distinction of possible commands.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu al-Husayn's third proof for necessary agency knowledge, based on how people request actions from others and their natural distinction between possible and impossible commands.

Core Idea
When we request an action from someone, we know necessarily that they are the one who will bring it about, and we distinguish between feasible and infeasible commands.

The third and final proof appeals to everyday social interactions. When we ask someone to do something, we do so knowing that they are the agent who will produce the action. We use gentle persuasion, express wonder, and show disapproval—all based on the assumption of their agency. Moreover, we naturally distinguish between commanding someone to stand or sit (which they can do) and commanding them to create the sky (which they cannot). This distinction would not exist without necessary knowledge of agency. In this lesson, we will read the third proof and its conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the third proof
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on the description of how people treat others when requesting actions: they use gentleness, forbidding, chiding, etc.
The proof is based on observable behavior, not abstract reasoning.
2
Understand the argument from request behavior
The way we request actions shows we know the other is the agent. We use different strategies because we know the action depends on their will.
This is similar to the first proof but from an outward perspective.
3
Analyze the distinction between possible and impossible commands
We intuitively know the difference between commanding someone to stand and commanding them to create the sky. This distinction requires necessary knowledge of our own agency and its limits.
The example is stark: commanding to create the sky is absurd because we know we are not agents of such things.
4
Read the concluding statement
After the three proofs, the author concludes that these three proofs indicate the necessary knowledge is obtained, and this is the final presentation of Abu al-Husayn's doctrine.
The conclusion ties the three proofs together.

حكاية قيل من يقول: العلم الضروري حاصل بأن العبد موجد. قد نقلنا في أول المسألة: أن أبا الحسين يدعي العلم الضروري بأن العبد موجد الأفعال نفسه. واحتجوا على أن العلم الضروري حاصل بذلك بوجوه ثلاثة:

الأول: إن كل عاقل يعلم من نفسه: أن وقوع تصرفاته موقوف على دواعيه، ولولا دواعيه لما وقع شيء منها. فمتى اشتد به الجوع، وكان تناول الطعام ممكناً، فإنه يصدر منه تناول الطعام. ومتى اعتقد أن في الطعام سما، ينصرف عنه. وكذلك يعلم من غيره من العقلاء، الذي أحوالهم سليمة: أنه يجب وقوع أفعالهم بحسب دواعيهم. فإن الولد العاقل السليم إذا علم ما له من المنفعة في شرب الماء، حال شدة عطشه، ولم يمنعه منه مانع، فإنه يصدر منه الشرب، لا محالة. ومتى علم ما له من المضرة في دخول النار، فإنه لا يدخل فيها. وإذا كان معنى الموجد للشيء: هو الذي يحدث منه الفعل، موافقاً لدواعيه، ثبت أن العقلاء قد يعرفون ببدائه عقولهم كون العبد كذلك. علمنا: أن علمهم بكون العبيد محدثين لتصرفاتهم: علم ضروري.

الوجه الثاني في تقرير ذلك: أن العقلاء يعلمون بالضرورة: حسن ذم من أساء إليهم، ومدح المحسن إليهم. ويعلمون بالضرورة: قبح المدح والذم على كونه طويلاً أو قصيراً، وكون السماء فوقه والأرض تحته. ولولا علمهم الضروري بكون العبد محدثاً لأفعاله، لما علموا بالضرورة حسن مدحه وذمه على أفعاله، وقبح مدحه وذمه على ما لا يكون واقعاً به. لأن العلم بحسن المدح والذم لما كان فرعاً على العلم بحدوث ذلك منهم ـ والعلم بالفرع إذا كان ضرورياً، كان العلم بالأصل أولى أن يكون ضروريا - لزم القطع بأن علم العقلاء بكونهم محدثين لتصرفاتهم: علم ضروري بديهي. ولأجل ظهور هذا العلم عند العقلاء، حصل هذا العلم للمراهقين. فإن الإنسان إذا رمى المراهق بأجرة، فإنه يلم الرامي ولا يذم تلك الأجرة. ولولا أنه عالم بالضرورة: بأن الرامي فاعل، وأن الأجرة ما فعلت شيئاً، وإلا لما فرق بينهما من الوجه المذكور.

الثالث: إن الواحد منا يجد من نفسه - ومن غيره ـ إذا طلب فعلا من غیره، أنه يطلب منه طلب عالم بأنه هو الذي يحدث ذلك الفعل. ولذلك فإنه يتلطف في استدعائه ذلك الفعل منه، بكل لطف وينهاه عن فعل ما يكرهه، ويعنفه على فعله، ويتعجب من فعله، ويستظرفه، ويعجب العقلاء في فعله. ويعلل كل ذلك: بأن فعله. وأيضاً: فنجد من أنفسنا: الفرق الضروري بين أمره بالقيام والقعود، وبين أمره بإيجاد السماء والكواكب. ولولا أن العلم الضروري حاصل بكوننا موجدين أفعالنا، وإلا لما صح ذلك.

قالوا: فهذه الوجوه الثلاثة: منبهة على أن العلم الضروري حاصل يكون العبد موجداً. وهذا غاية تقرير مذهب أبي الحسين البصري، في هذه المسألة.
Account of what is said: The necessary knowledge is obtained that the servant is the agent. We have reported at the beginning of the issue: that Abu al-Husayn claims the necessary knowledge that the servant is the creator of the actions himself. And they argued that the necessary knowledge is obtained thereby by three proofs:

First: Every rational person knows from himself that the occurrence of his actions depends on his motives, and without his motives none of them would occur. So when hunger intensifies for him, and eating food is possible, then eating food issues from him. And when he believes that there is poison in the food, he turns away from it. Similarly, he knows from other rational persons whose states are sound that the occurrence of their actions must be according to their motives. For a rational, sound child, if he knows the benefit in drinking water when his thirst is severe, and no preventer prevents him, then drinking issues from him, inevitably. And if he knows the harm in entering fire, he does not enter it. And if the meaning of 'agent of a thing' is: he from whom the action occurs, in accordance with his motives, it is established that rational persons know by the first principles of their intellects that the servant is such. We know that their knowledge that servants are originators of their actions is necessary knowledge.

Second proof in establishing that: Rational persons know necessarily the goodness of blaming one who does evil to them, and praising the one who does good to them. And they know necessarily the ugliness of praise and blame for being tall or short, and for the sky being above them and the earth beneath them. And if they did not have necessary knowledge that the servant is the originator of his actions, they would not know necessarily the goodness of praising and blaming him for his actions, and the ugliness of praising and blaming him for what does not occur from him. Because knowledge of the goodness of praise and blame, since it is a branch of the knowledge of the occurrence of that from them—and knowledge of the branch, if it is necessary, then knowledge of the root is more worthy of being necessary—it necessarily follows that the knowledge of rational persons that they are originators of their actions is necessary and intuitive. And because of the manifestation of this knowledge among rational persons, this knowledge is obtained by adolescents. For if a person throws a stone at an adolescent, he blames the thrower and does not blame that stone. And if he were not aware necessarily that the thrower is an agent and that the stone did nothing, he would not differentiate between them in the mentioned respect.

Third: Each one of us finds from himself—and from others—when he requests an action from another, that he requests it from him as one who knows that he is the one who brings about that action. Therefore, he is gentle in soliciting that action from him, in every gentleness, and forbids him from what he dislikes, and chides him for his action, and wonders at his action, and considers it elegant, and rational persons marvel at his action. And he explains all that: by it being his action. Also: we find from ourselves the necessary distinction between commanding him to stand and sit, and between commanding him to create the sky and the planets. And if the necessary knowledge that we are agents of our actions were not obtained, that would not be correct.

They said: So these three proofs indicate that the necessary knowledge is obtained that the servant is the agent. And this is the ultimate presentation of the doctrine of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, in this issue.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage presents three proofs advanced by Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and his followers to establish that human beings have necessary (daruri) knowledge that they are the agents (mujidun) of their own actions. The author begins by stating the claim: necessary knowledge exists that the servant (al-ʿabd) is the agent. He then reports that Abu al-Husayn claims this knowledge, and they argue for it with three proofs.

The first proof relies on the human experience of acting according to motives (dawaʿi). Every rational person knows from himself that his actions depend on his motives: when hungry, he eats; when he believes food is poisoned, he refrains. Similarly, he observes this in other rational persons. Since the meaning of 'agent' (mujid) is one from whom an action proceeds in accordance with motives, rational people know by the first principles of their intellects that the servant is such. Therefore, their knowledge that servants are originators of their actions is necessary.

The second proof focuses on the universal human practice of praising and blaming. Rational people necessarily know that it is good to blame one who harms them and praise one who benefits them. They also necessarily know that it is ugly to praise or blame someone for involuntary traits like height or the position of the sky and earth. If they did not have necessary knowledge that the servant originates his actions, they would not make this distinction. Since knowledge of the goodness of praise and blame (a branch) is necessary, the knowledge that humans are agents (the root) must be even more necessary. This is illustrated by the example of a person who throws a stone at an adolescent: the adolescent blames the thrower, not the stone, showing he necessarily knows the thrower is the agent.

The third proof appeals to the way people interact when requesting actions. When someone asks another to do something, he does so as one who knows that the other is the one who will bring about that action. He uses gentle persuasion, forbids disliked actions, chides, wonders, and praises—all based on the assumption that the action belongs to the other. Moreover, people intuitively distinguish between commanding someone to stand or sit (which he can do) and commanding him to create the sky and planets (which he cannot). This distinction would not exist without the necessary knowledge that we are agents of our actions.

In conclusion, the three proofs together indicate that the necessary knowledge that the servant is the agent is indeed obtained, and this is the final presentation of Abu al-Husayn's doctrine on this issue.

Key Terms
علم ضروري
موجد
فاعل
دواعي

Checkpoint

C) The role of motives in action
D) The idea that God is the sole agent

Key Takeaway
The third proof shows that our everyday interactions and intuitive distinctions confirm the necessary knowledge of human agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you truly embrace that you are the sole agent of that action, or do you find yourself shifting blame to others or circumstances? How would owning your agency change your repentance?
?
Think of a time you treated a command as impossible or made an excuse for not acting. Did you act as if you were not an agent? What would it mean to live according to the necessary knowledge that you are the one who brings about your actions?
?
In your interactions with others, do you genuinely treat them as agents responsible for their choices, or do you subtly deny their agency by controlling or enabling? How can you honor their agency as this passage demands?

Second Proof: Praise and Blame
Presents the opponent's second proof from universal praise and blame.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the universal practice of praise and blame presupposes and thus proves that human agency is known necessarily.

Core Idea
Praise and blame only make sense if we already know humans cause their actions.

In this passage, the opponent presents a second argument for necessary knowledge of human agency. Instead of relying on direct introspection, the argument appeals to universal human practices: praising and blaming others for their actions. The reasoning shows that such practices would be irrational unless people already have necessary knowledge that humans originate their own deeds. As you read, pay attention to the logical structure: if a subsidiary (praise/blame) is known necessarily, its principle (agency) must be even more necessarily known.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow: the opponent states a universal premise, draws a logical conclusion, and then gives an example.
The argument uses the terms 'ضروري' (necessary) and 'بديهي' (self-evident).
2
Identify the universal premise and the inference
The opponent claims that all rational people know necessarily that it is good to blame a wrongdoer and praise a benefactor. They also know it is bad to praise/blame based on incidental traits. From this, they infer that the knowledge of human agency must be necessary, because the knowledge of praise/blame is subsidiary to it.
This is a logical argument from the subsidiary (far') to the principle (asl).
3
Examine the concrete example
The example of the hired worker and the wage illustrates the point: people naturally blame the worker, not the wage, showing they necessarily distinguish the agent from the non-agent.
The word 'المراهق' here means 'adolescent' or 'young hired person', not 'adult'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني في تقرير ذلك : أن العقلاء يعلمون بالضرورة : حسن ذم من أساء إليهم ، ومدح المحسن إليهم. ويعلمون بالضرورة : قبح المدح والذم على كونه طويلاً أو قصيراً ، وكون السماء فوقه والأرض تحته . ولولا علمهم الضروري بكون العبد محدثاً لأفعاله ، لما علموا بالضرورة حسن مدحه وذمه على أفعاله ، وقبح مدحه وذمه على ما لا يكون واقعاً به . لأن العلم بحسن المدح والذم لما كان فرعاً على العلم بحدوث ذلك منهم ـ والعلم بالفرع إذا كان ضرورياً ، كان العلم بالأصل أولى أن يكون ضروريا - لزم القطع بأن علم العقلاء بكونهم محدثين لتصرفاتهم : علم ضروري بديهي . ولأجل ظهور هذا العلم عند العقلاء ، حصل هذا العلم للمراهقين . فإن الإنسان إذا رمى المراهق بأجرة ، فإنه يلم الرامي ولا يذم تلك الأجرة . ولولا أنه عالم بالضرورة : بأن الرامي فاعل ، وأن الأجرة ما فعلت شيئاً ، وإلا لما فرق بينهما من الوجه المذكور .
The second aspect in establishing that: that rational people know necessarily: the goodness of blaming one who wrongs them, and praising the one who does good to them. And they know necessarily: the badness of praise and blame based on being tall or short, and the sky being above them and the earth beneath them. And were it not for their necessary knowledge that the servant originates his actions, they would not know necessarily the goodness of praising and blaming him for his actions, and the badness of praising and blaming him for what does not occur from him. Because the knowledge of the goodness of praise and blame, since it is subsidiary to the knowledge that those [actions] occur from them — and the knowledge of the subsidiary, if it is necessary, then the knowledge of the principle is more fitting to be necessary — it follows conclusively that the rational people's knowledge that they are originators of their actions is necessary and self-evident knowledge. And due to the manifestness of this knowledge among rational people, this knowledge is acquired by adolescents. For if a person hires an adolescent with a wage, he blames the hired person and does not blame that wage. And were it not that he knows necessarily that the hired person is an agent, and that the wage did nothing, otherwise he would not distinguish between them in the mentioned respect.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage presents the opponent's second proof that knowledge of human agency is necessary (daruri). The argument begins by stating that rational people universally know that it is good to blame someone who wrongs them and to praise someone who does good. They also know that it is bad to praise or blame someone based on incidental attributes like height or position. The opponent argues that this universal practice of praising and blaming actions only makes sense if people necessarily know that the person being praised or blamed is the actual originator (muhdith) of the action. If people did not have such necessary knowledge, they would not have the corresponding necessary knowledge of the goodness or badness of praise and blame. The logic is that knowledge of a subsidiary (far') that is necessary implies that knowledge of the principle (asl) is even more necessarily known. Therefore, the knowledge that humans originate their own actions must be necessary and self-evident (badihi). The proof is reinforced by an example: when someone hires a person (the 'adolescent' or worker) for a wage, they naturally blame the worker if something goes wrong, not the wage itself. This distinction shows that the blamer necessarily knows that the worker is the agent and the wage is not. Thus, the argument concludes that this knowledge is innate and universal.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is about the moral justification of praise and blame, claiming that all praise and blame are justified.
Correction: The argument is not about morality but about the necessary epistemological belief that underlies the very act of praising or blaming. It claims that such acts presuppose a necessary belief in human agency, regardless of whether the praise or blame is actually deserved.

Checkpoint

A) Because the wage is valuable, so it is praised.
B) Because the worker is blamed and the wage is not, showing a necessary distinction between agent and non-agent.
C) Because the worker is tall and the wage is short.
D) Because the wage is an object and cannot act.

Key Takeaway
The universal practice of praising and blaming human actions reveals that humans necessarily know themselves as originators of their deeds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
You separate agents from non-agents in daily life—like blaming your waiter for a mistake, not the plate. How does this ingrained distinction confront your excuses for your own sins?
?
If the knowledge that you are the originator of your actions is self-evident, why do you often act as if you are not responsible for your choices? What would change if you truly lived by this necessary knowledge?

The Introspective Proof for Human Agency
Presents the opponent's third proof from introspective awareness of agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's introspective argument that necessary knowledge of human agency is derived from our everyday commands and distinctions.

Core Idea
Our natural commands reveal a necessary awareness of agency.

In this passage, the opponent presents a third proof that our knowledge of being the originators of our actions is necessary. He appeals to introspection: observe how you yourself command others. You request actions with the knowledge that the other person is the one who will perform them. This awareness is reflected in your tone and responses. Moreover, you naturally distinguish between feasible commands (stand up) and impossible ones (create planets). This distinction would not be possible unless you necessarily know yourself to be the originator of your actions. Let's examine the argument in detail.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the literal translation. Let the opponent's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the two components
The opponent gives two supporting observations: (a) the requester's behavior (gentleness, rebuke, etc.) shows they believe the other originates the action; (b) the necessary distinction between commands within power and those beyond. Identify both in the text.
The first part is about the requester's attitude, the second about the scope of commands.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The opponent claims that were it not for necessary knowledge of our own agency, these phenomena would not be sound. Ask yourself: does this argument prove necessary knowledge, or could it be acquired?
This is a key point for later critical analysis.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إن الواحد منا يجد من نفسه - ومن غيره ـ إذا طلب فعلا من غیره ، أنه يطلب منه طلب عالم بأنه هو الذي يحدث ذلك الفعل . ولذلك فإنه يتلطف في استدعائه ذلك الفعل منه ، بكل لطف وينهاه عن فعل ما يكرهه ، ويعنفه على فعله ، ويتعجب من فعله ، ويستظرفه ، ويعجب العقلاء في فعله . ويعلل كل ذلك : بأن فعله . وأيضاً : فنجد من أنفسنا : الفرق الضروري بين أمره بالقيام والقعود ، وبين أمره بإيجاد السماء والكواكب . ولولا أن العلم الضروري حاصل بكوننا موجدين الأفعالنا ، وإلا لما صح ذلك
The third: that each one of us finds from himself — and from others — when he requests an action from another, that he requests it with a request knowing that it is he who originates that action. Therefore he is gentle in soliciting that action from him, with all gentleness, and forbids him from doing what he dislikes, and rebukes him for his action, and marvels at his action, and considers it clever, and rational people are amazed at his action. And he explains all that: as being his action. Also: we find from ourselves the necessary distinction between commanding someone to stand and sit, and commanding him to bring into existence the sky and the planets. And were it not that necessary knowledge is obtained that we are originators of our actions, otherwise that would not be sound.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage presents the opponent's third proof that knowledge of human agency is necessary. The argument is introspective: each person finds that when they request an action from someone else, they do so with the awareness that the other person is the one who will originate (yuhdith) that action. This awareness manifests in various behaviors: the requester uses persuasion, forbids disliked actions, rebukes, marvels, or praises the action, all of which presuppose that the action truly belongs to the agent. Additionally, the opponent points out a necessary distinction we all make: we naturally distinguish between commanding someone to do something within their power (like standing or sitting) and commanding them to do something impossible (like creating the heavens and planets). This distinction would not be made unless we necessarily know ourselves to be originators (mujidun) of our actions. Thus, the opponent concludes that this knowledge is necessary and universal.

Key Terms
محدث (muhdith)
موجد (mujid)
فرق ضروري (farq daruri)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is about whether it is permissible to command someone to do something.
Correction: The argument is actually about the psychological awareness of agency that is revealed in our commands and reactions, not about the ethics of commanding.

Checkpoint

C) Commands within human power and commands to create the universe.
D) Commands given gently and commands given harshly.

Key Takeaway
The opponent argues that our natural way of commanding and reacting reveals a necessary awareness that we are the originators of our actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Think of a time you blamed circumstances for your own failure to act. Did that blame align with the necessary awareness the lesson describes—that you are the originator of your actions? How might that awareness change how you respond next time?
?
The lesson says we naturally distinguish between commanding someone to stand and commanding them to create the heavens. Do you make that distinction in your daily expectations of yourself? What does that reveal about your belief in your own agency?

First Refutation: Historical Disagreement Proves Non-Necessity
Refutes the claim of necessity by citing historical disagreement among scholars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the historical disagreement among early scholars to refute the claim that knowledge of human agency is necessary.

Core Idea
Disagreement proves no necessity; necessary truths are universally recognized.

The author begins his refutation by pointing to a simple historical fact: before Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, scholars were divided on the issue of human agency. If knowledge of this were self-evident, everyone would agree. The fact that they did not—and even the Mu'tazilites relied on proofs—shows it cannot be necessary. This lesson walks through that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Pay attention to the logical structure: the author states the claim is false, then gives the first proof.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the two groups
Note the two camps before Abu al-Husayn: those who say actions are created by God, and those who say actions are originated by the servant. Both used inferential reasoning.
This historical fact is central to the argument.
3
Understand 'necessary' vs 'inferential'
Recognize the key terms: daruri (necessary, self-evident) and istidlali (inferential, based on proof). The author argues that the Mu'tazilite shaykhs all used proofs, so they did not consider this knowledge necessary.
4
Follow the implication
If the knowledge were necessary, then all who disagreed would be denying self-evident truths (badihiyyat). That is absurd, so the claim of necessity is false.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.
5
Conclude the refutation
Since the implication leads to an impossibility, the original claim (that knowledge of human agency is necessary) is refuted. This is the first of several proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أن دعوى الضرورة في كون العبد موجداً لأفعاله : باطل ويدل عليه وجوه . الأول : إن الناس كانوا قبل أبي الحسين فريقان : منهم من يزعم أن فعل العبد مخلوق الله تعالى، ومنهم من يزعم : أنه يحصل بإيجاد العبد وتكوينه . والعلم بذلك علم استدلالي. ولذلك فإن جميع مشايخ المعتزلة إنما كانوا يثبتون هذا المطلوب بناء على الدلائل والبينات . ولو كان العلم بهذا المطلوب ضرورياً ، لكان جميع الناس الذين كانوا قبل أبي الحسين ، منكرين للبديهيات . أما مخالفوا المعتزلة . فلا شك أنه يلزم على قول أبي الحسين كونهم منكرين للبديهيات. وأما جميع مشايخ المعتزلة فكذلك . لأنهم لما كانوا مطبقين على أنه لا سبيل إلى إثبات هذا المطلوب إلا بالدلائل ، كانوا متفقين على أن العلم بهذا المطلوب ليس بضروري . فثبت : أن العلم بهذا المطلوب لو كان ضرورياً ، لزم أن يقال : إن جميع الخلق الذين كانوا قبل أبي الحسين ، كانوا مطبقين متفقين على إنكار البديهيات والضروريات . ولما كان ذلك باطلاً . علمنا فساد قول أبي الحسين .
Know: that the claim of necessity regarding the servant being the originator of his actions is false, and evidence for that are aspects. The first: that people before Abu al-Husayn were two groups: some claim that the servant's action is created by God Almighty, and some claim that it occurs by the servant's origination and formation. And knowledge of that is inferential knowledge. Therefore all the shaykhs of the Mu'tazila used to affirm this sought outcome based on proofs and evidences. And if knowledge of this sought outcome were necessary, then all the people who were before Abu al-Husayn would be deniers of self-evidents. As for the opponents of the Mu'tazila, there is no doubt that on Abu al-Husayn's statement it necessarily follows that they are deniers of self-evidents. And as for all the shaykhs of the Mu'tazila, likewise. Because since they were in agreement that there is no path to affirming this sought outcome except through proofs, they were unanimous that knowledge of this sought outcome is not necessary. So it is established: that if knowledge of this sought outcome were necessary, it would necessarily follow that all creatures who were before Abu al-Husayn were unanimously in agreement on the possibility of denying self-evidents and necessities. And since that is false, we know the corruption of Abu al-Husayn's statement.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage begins the author's refutation of the claim that knowledge of human agency is necessary (daruri). The author states outright that this claim is false and will provide proofs. The first proof is historical: before Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, scholars were divided into two camps — those who held that actions are created by God (the Jabriyya/Ash'ariyya) and those who held that actions occur by the servant's own origination (the Mu'tazila). Both sides arrived at their positions through inferential reasoning (istidlal), not through immediate necessity. The author points out that all the leading Mu'tazilite scholars (mashayikh al-mu'tazila) established their position based on proofs and evidences, which would be unnecessary if the knowledge were necessary. If the knowledge were truly necessary, then all people before Abu al-Husayn who disagreed would be denying self-evident truths (badihiyyat), which is absurd. Even the Mu'tazilites agreed among themselves that the only way to prove this point is through proofs, implying it is not necessary. Therefore, the claim of necessity leads to the impossible conclusion that all earlier humans denied self-evident truths. Since this is false, Abu al-Husayn's position is refuted.

Key Terms
ضروري (daruri)
استدلالي (istidlali)
بديهيات (badihiyyat)
مجبّر (jabbar)
معتزلة (mu'tazila)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument requires that all people agree on the truth of human agency.
Correction: The argument only requires that a necessary truth would be universally recognized as such; the fact of disagreement itself disproves necessity.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the Mu'tazilite shaykhs before Abu al-Husayn did not consider knowledge of human agency to be necessary?
A) Because they denied human agency entirely.
B) Because they relied on proofs to establish it.
C) Because they were unaware of the issue.
D) Because they followed the Ash'ari position.

Key Takeaway
Historical disagreement among scholars proves that knowledge of human agency is not necessary, but inferential.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a firm belief about your own free will or responsibility, do you treat it as a self-evident truth that needs no examination? Have you ever subjected it to the same scrutiny as the scholars in this passage did?
?
If even the greatest Mu'tazilite scholars disagreed among themselves and arrived at their conclusions through proofs, how can you claim certainty on such matters without engaging with the evidence?
?
Is there a belief you hold that you've never questioned because it feels 'obvious'? What would it take for you to treat it as inferential rather than necessary, and how might that change your humility before God and His servants?

Introspection and the Unknown Cause
Begins the second refutation arguing that introspection shows uncertainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why introspection alone cannot provide necessary knowledge of human causation.

Core Idea
We know our actions follow intentions, but not what actually causes them.

The author continues his refutation of the claim that human agency is known with necessity. He asks us to turn inward and examine what we actually know when we act. The passage reveals a crucial gap in our self-knowledge: we are certain of the correlation between intention and action, but uncertain about the causal mechanism. This uncertainty undermines the claim of necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice that the passage is incomplete; it cuts off mid-sentence.
The argument is cut short, but the key point is made before the interruption.
2
Identify the distinction between known and unknown
The passage states that we do know actions occur in accordance with our mental conceptions and motivations. But what we do not know is the actual effective cause (mu'aththir) that brings the action into existence.
This distinction is the core of the argument.
3
Examine the three possibilities for causation
The author lists three possible accounts of the effective cause: our power alone, our power combined with motivations, or something else concurrent with our intention. He declares that which one is true is 'absolutely unknown'.
This shows that certainty about our own agency is impossible.
4
Consider the 'custom of Allah' hypothesis
The author then suggests a plausible alternative: that God has made it His habit (jara 'adathu) to create the action when a person has sound limbs and intends it. This is presented as not unlikely, further undermining the claim of necessity.
The passage cuts off before completing the scenario, but the thrust is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إنا إذا رجعنا إلى أنفسنا ، لم نعلم إلا أن هذه الأفعال حاصلة على وفق تصورنا ودواعينا . فأما أن المؤثر في دخولها في الوجود هو قدرتنا فقط ، أو مجموع قدرتنا مع دواعينا . أو المؤثر في حصولها شيء آخر يحصلها مقارناً لذلك المقصود فذلك البتة غير معلوم . إذ ليس من المشتبه أن يقال : إنه تعالى أجرى عادته بأن الإنسان الذي تكون أعضاؤه سليمة ، ومزاج بدنه يكون خالياً من الأسقام والأمراض ، إذا خلق فيه إرادة حصول شيء ، فإنه
The second: that if we turn to ourselves, we know only that these actions occur in accordance with our conception and motivations. As for whether the effective cause of their coming into existence is our power alone, or the combination of our power with our motivations, or the effective cause of their occurrence is something else that brings them about concurrent with that intended purpose — that is absolutely unknown. Since it is not unlikely that it be said: He, exalted, has made it His custom that when a person whose limbs are sound and whose bodily temperament is free from ailments and diseases, if He creates in him an intention to obtain something, then he...
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage presents the beginning of the author's second refutation argument against the claim that knowledge of human agency is necessary. The argument appeals to introspection: when we examine ourselves, we only know that our actions occur in accordance with our mental conceptions (tasawwur) and motivations (dawa'in). However, we do not know with certainty what the actual effective cause (mu'aththir) of these actions is. Is it our power (qudra) alone? Or the combination of our power and motivations? Or is it something else (i.e., God's creation) that produces the action concurrently with our intention? The author asserts that this is absolutely not known. He then suggests that it is not implausible to say that God has instituted a custom (jara 'adathu) that when a person has sound limbs and a healthy body, and God creates in him a will to obtain something, then [the action occurs by God's creation]. The passage cuts off before completing the scenario, but the point is clear: we cannot be certain that we are the independent originators of our actions, and therefore the knowledge is not necessary. This argument is incomplete in the current text, continuing likely on the next page.

Key Terms
مؤثر (mu'aththir)
قدرة (qudra)
دواعي (dawa'i)
عادة اللـه (ādat Allāh)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies that humans have any role in their actions.
Correction: The argument does not deny that we have some role; it argues that the exact causal mechanism is unknown, so necessary knowledge is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to this argument, what do we NOT know with certainty when we introspect about our actions?
A) That our actions exist.
B) That we have motivations.
C) What the effective cause of the action is.
D) That our limbs are sound.

Key Takeaway
Through introspection, we cannot be certain of the true cause of our actions, so the knowledge of human agency is not necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an action, do you ever pause to consider that you might not be the one truly causing it? How would that change your sense of pride or humility before Allah?
?
If you cannot be certain that your own power alone brings about your actions, how does that affect your reliance on Allah in every moment? What would it look like to live with that constant awareness?

Introspection and the Limits of Causal Knowledge
Author uses introspection to show we only know constant conjunction, not causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why introspection only reveals constant conjunction (muqarana) between our will and actions, not causal efficacy.

Core Idea
Introspection shows we only know actions accompany our will, not that our will causes them.

This passage presents a second proof challenging the notion that we have direct intuitive knowledge of causing our actions. The author invites us to examine our own experience and see what we really know about the relationship between our will and our actions. What we find is only a constant conjunction, not a necessary causal connection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument flow before interpreting.
Notice the author's use of 'إلا أن' to limit what we know.
2
Identify the three possibilities for causation
The author lists three alternatives for what brings actions into existence: our power alone, power plus motives, or something else. Find these in the text.
The third option is 'مؤثر في حصولها شيء آخر يحصلها مقارناً لذلك المقصود'.
3
Understand the conclusion and the role of God's habit
The author concludes that only the concomitance of action with power and will is known, not causation. He then explains that God's habit (ʻādah) is the actual cause.
The term 'عادة' is key: it refers to God's custom, not our power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إنا إذا رجعنا إلى أنفسنا لم نعلم إلا أن هذه الأفعال حاصلة على وفق تصورنا ودواعينا . فأما أن المؤثر في دخولها في الوجود هو قدرتنا فقط أو مجموع قدرتنا مع دواعينا أو المؤثر في حصولها شيء آخر يحصلها مقارناً لذلك المقصود فذلك البتة غير معلوم . إذ ليس من المشتبه أن يقال : إنه تعالى أجرى عادته بأن الإنسان الذي تكون أعضاؤه سليمة ومزاج بدنه يكون خالياً من الأسقام والأمراض إذا خلق فيه إرادة حصول شيء فإنه يخلق ذلك المراد على وفق تلك الإرادة. والحاصل : أن مقارنة الفعل مع حصول القدرة والإرادة معلومة . فأما كون الفعل به فذاك البتة غير معلوم . ومن قال خلاف ذلك كان مكابراً معانداً
Second: When we examine ourselves, we only know that these actions occur in accordance with our conception and motives. As for whether the effecting factor in their coming into existence is solely our power or the totality of our power together with our motives, or the effecting factor in their occurrence is something else which causes them to occur concurrent with that intended [action], that is absolutely unknown. For it is not inconceivable to say that God Almighty has made it His habit that a human being whose limbs are sound and whose bodily temperament is free from illnesses and diseases, if He creates in him a will for the occurrence of something, He creates that willed thing in accordance with that will. In summary: the concomitance of the action with the occurrence of power and will is known. As for the action being by means of it, that is absolutely unknown. Whoever says otherwise is an obdurate opponent.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof against the necessary knowledge of human agency. The proof is based on introspection. The author argues that when we examine ourselves, we only know that actions occur in accordance with our conceptions and motives. However, we do not know whether the effective cause is solely our power, or our power together with motives, or something else. The only knowledge we have is of the constant conjunction between power/will and action. This conjunction is due to God's habit, not because the action is caused by our power. The author emphasizes that claiming otherwise is mere obstinacy. This argument challenges the notion that we have direct intuitive knowledge of causing our actions.

Key Terms
قدرة
إرادة
عادة
مؤثر
مقارنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: We intuitively know that our will directly causes our actions.
Correction: Introspection only reveals a constant conjunction between will and action; the actual cause is unknown and is attributed to God's habit.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, what is the result of introspection regarding our actions?
A) We know with certainty that our power creates the action
B) We only know the constant conjunction of power/will and action, not causation
C) We know that God directly creates all actions
D) We know that we are free agents

Key Takeaway
This proof shows that our knowledge of the relationship between our will and actions is limited to constant conjunction, not causal efficacy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If the relationship between your will and action is only a constant conjunction, not causal, how should that affect your sense of ownership and pride in your deeds?
?

beginner

Will's Independence from Prior Will
Introspective argument avoiding infinite regress, concluding compulsion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the will for an action does not depend on another will, and that this leads to the conclusion that humans are compelled even in the guise of choice.

Core Idea
The will is not willed; otherwise infinite regress. We are compelled in the form of choice.

The author invites us to introspect: when we will something, do we also will that will? Our immediate awareness tells us no. This simple observation blocks an infinite regress and reveals a deeper truth: our will arises necessarily, making us compelled agents despite the feeling of choice. Let's read the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style.
2
Understand the introspective argument
The author says we know by reflecting on ourselves that our will for something does not depend on our willing that will. Why would that lead to infinite regress? Think about it.
3
Grasp the conclusion: compulsion in guise of choice
The author concludes that whether we will it or not, we will that specific action, and this shows man is compelled (مضطر) even when he thinks he chooses. Reflect on what this means for human responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إنا إذا رجعنا إلى أنفسنا ، علمنا : أن إرادتنا للشيء ، لا تتوقف على إرادتنا لتلك الإرادة . وإلا لزم التسلسل : بل نعلم بالضرورة : أنا إن شئنا أم أبينا ، فإنا نريد ذلك الفعل المخصوص ، وتعلم أنه متى حصلت تلك الإرادة بنا . فالإنسان مضطر في صورة نختار . والعجب من أبي الحسين أنه خالف أصحابه في قولهم : « الفعل لا يتوقف على الداعي ، وزعم : أن الفعل يتوقف على الداعي ، وزعم أن حصول الفعل عقيب الداعي واجب. والاعتراف بهاتين المقدمتين : عين الاعتراف بالجبر . ثم إنه بعد ذلك بالغ في إثبات الاعتزال ، وزعم : أن العلم الضروري حاصل بكون العبد موجداً . والجمع بين هذين القولين : عجيب . والأظهر : أنه لما بالغ في تقرير الجبر من حيث المعني ، فلعله خاف من أصحابه ، فأراد إخفاء ذلك المذهب بهذه المبالغة . والله أعلم .
The third: When we reflect upon ourselves, we know that our willing of something does not depend on our willing of that will. Otherwise an infinite regress would follow. Rather we know by necessity that whether we will it or not, we will that specific action, and we know that whenever that will occurs in us, then man is compelled ( مضطر) in the form of choice. And astonishing about Abu al-Husayn is that he opposed his companions in their statement: 'The act does not depend on the motive (al-dā'ī),' and he claimed that the act depends on the motive, and claimed that the occurrence of the act following the motive is necessary. And acknowledgment of these two premises is precisely acknowledgment of compulsion (jabr). Then after that he went to extremes in establishing Mu'tazilism, and claimed that necessary knowledge is attained that the servant is an originator (mūjid). The combination of these two statements is astonishing. The most apparent is that when he went to extremes in establishing compulsion in terms of meaning, perhaps he feared his companions, so he wanted to conceal that doctrine by this exaggeration. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage contains an argument (the third point) and a critique. The argument begins by directing us to introspection: when we examine ourselves, we know that our will for something does not depend on a prior will for that will. If it did, an infinite regress (التسلسل) would arise. Instead, we know by necessity that regardless of our choice, we will that specific act, and whenever that will occurs in us, we are compelled (مضطر) despite the appearance of choice. This shows that human beings are compelled even while they think they choose. Then the author expresses astonishment at Abu al-Husayn (presumably Abu al-Husayn al-Basri or a Mu'tazilite figure) who opposed his own school. The Mu'tazilites held that the act does not depend on an external motive (الداعي); but Abu al-Husayn claimed that the act does depend on the motive and that the occurrence of the act after the motive is necessary (واجب). The author argues that admitting these two premises is precisely admitting determinism (الجبر). Yet Abu al-Husayn also went to extremes in defending Mu'tazilism, claiming that necessary knowledge tells us that the servant (العبد) is the originator (موجد) of his actions. The author finds this combination contradictory and suggests that Abu al-Husayn, having strongly argued for determinism, may have feared his companions and tried to hide his true doctrine with exaggeration. The passage ends with 'والله أعلم'.

Key Terms
الإرادة
التسلسل
الضرورة
مضطر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if the will is not willed, then human responsibility is preserved because we choose freely.
Correction: The author argues that the will not being willed shows we are compelled (مضطر) even in the guise of choice, because the will arises necessarily without our prior will.

Checkpoint
What does the third argument conclude about the human will?
A) The will is free and self-caused.
B) The will depends on a prior will.
C) The will is necessary and man is compelled in the guise of choice.
D) The will is independent of motives.

Key Takeaway
The will does not depend on another will; this forces the conclusion that humans are compelled agents, though they appear to choose.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recall a major decision you made recently, can you honestly identify a conscious moment where you 'willed your will'? Or did the desire simply arise in you? How does this lesson challenge the way you think about your own responsibility?
?
The author says man is 'compelled in the form of choice.' Does that thought make you feel relieved or uneasy? If your will is not ultimately in your control, how does that change the way you approach your daily worship and reliance on Allah?
?
If every action you will is necessitated, what does that mean for your sin? Can you truly repent if your will to repent is itself not willed? Let this question drive you to genuine humility before the Creator.

Critique of Abu al-Husayn's Contradictory Position
Critique of Abu al-Husayn's contradictory statements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu al-Husayn's contradictory stance on determinism and free will, and see how acknowledging necessity leads to admitting compulsion.

Core Idea
Abu al-Husayn admitted determinism but then exaggerated free will—a contradiction.

After establishing the deterministic conclusion, the author turns to critique a Mu'tazilite scholar, Abu al-Husayn, who held contradictory positions. He admitted that acts depend on motives and are necessary, which is determinism, but then he also claimed the servant is the originator of actions. The author finds this astonishing and suggests Abu al-Husayn may have hidden his true beliefs. Let's read this critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once. Then the translation. Pay attention to the two claims attributed to Abu al-Husayn.
This is a critique, so note the author's tone.
2
Identify the two contradictory claims
First claim: the act depends on the motive (الداعي) and is necessary. Second claim: the servant is the originator (موجد) of his actions. Why are these contradictory?
3
Understand the author's analysis
The author says acknowledging the first two premises is 'عين الاعتراف بالجبر' (the very acknowledgment of compulsion). Then Abu al-Husayn went to extremes in Mu'tazilism. How can both be true? The author suggests he was hiding his determinist view.

This shows that human beings are compelled even while they think they choose. Then the author expresses astonishment at Abu al-Husayn (presumably Abu al-Husayn al-Basri or a Mu'tazilite figure) who opposed his own school. The Mu'tazilites held that the act does not depend on an external motive (الداعي); but Abu al-Husayn claimed that the act does depend on the motive and that the occurrence of the act after the motive is necessary (واجب). The author argues that admitting these two premises is precisely admitting determinism (الجبر). Yet Abu al-Husayn also went to extremes in defending Mu'tazilism, claiming that necessary knowledge tells us that the servant (العبد) is the originator (موجد) of his actions. The author finds this combination contradictory and suggests that Abu al-Husayn, having strongly argued for determinism, may have feared his companions and tried to hide his true doctrine with exaggeration. The passage ends with 'والله أعلم'.

Key Terms
الداعي
الجبر
موجد
مضطر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that believing in necessary causation from motives is compatible with free will.
Correction: The author states that accepting that the act depends on a motive and necessarily follows it is 'the very acknowledgment of compulsion' (عين الاعتراف بالجبر), which contradicts free will.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about acknowledging the two premises that act depends on motive and is necessary?
A) It is the basis of Mu'tazilism.
B) It is the very acknowledgment of compulsion.
C) It is compatible with free will.
D) It is a mistake.

Key Takeaway
Admitting that acts depend on motives and are necessary is equivalent to accepting determinism, making it contradictory to also claim humans are originators.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief about Allah’s control over your life that you later contradicted because you feared what others might think? What was that belief, and why did you hide it?
?
If you truly believed that every action of yours is necessitated by a motive that Allah creates, would you still feel responsible for your sins? How does this lesson challenge you to take ownership of your deeds?
?
When you find yourself defending a theological position just to fit in with a group, what does that reveal about the state of your heart? Are you more concerned with truth or with belonging?

The First Proof for Human Agency: Actions Follow Motives
Author presents first proof and explains first premise with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first premise of the proof for human agency: that human actions necessarily follow motives and aversions, as argued through concrete examples.

Core Idea
Human actions are necessitated by motives, not random — and this proves we are the agents.

This passage begins a new chapter presenting proofs that humans are the true originators of their actions. The first proof argues that because our actions must align with our motives and aversions, they must originate from us. The author begins by establishing the first premise: the necessary connection between actions and motives, illustrated by vivid examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall structure
Notice that this is part of a larger proof with two premises, but the text we have fully covers the first premise and begins the second. The second premise is incomplete due to a page cut-off.
The proof aims to show that if actions necessarily follow motives, the agent must be the human, not an external force.
2
Read the examples carefully
The author gives two examples: a fasting person in summer who drinks water when permitted, and a person who avoids fire knowing its pain. These illustrate that given strong motives and no deterrents, actions inevitably occur or are avoided.
Both examples assume perfect knowledge and no external constraints.
3
Understand the logical force
The examples are meant to show a necessary connection between motives and actions. If such a connection holds, then when we act, it is because our motives led us to act, implying we are the cause.
This is the first step in proving human agency; the second premise would complete the argument.
4
Note the incomplete second premise
The text cuts off after stating the second premise and beginning its evidence. The intended conclusion is that if the actions were caused by another, the connection to our motives would be irrelevant, so we must be the agents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني : حكاية الدلائل التي تمسكوا بها في إثبات أن العبد موجد . اعلم أنهم احتجوا بوجوه كثيرة : الحجة الأولى : أنهم قالوا : إن أفعالنا يجب وقوعها على وفق دواعينا ، ويجب انتفاؤها على حسب كراهاتنا لها . وذلك يدل على وقوعها بنا . أما المقدمة الأولى : فبيانها : أن الصائم في الصيف الصائف إذا اشتدت شهوته إلى شرب الماء ، والشارع يشير إليه بذلك ، والطبيب يشير إليه به ، وعلم قطعاً أنه لا تبعة عليه في شرب ذلك الماء ، في الحال ولا في الاستقبال ، فإنه لا بد وأن يشربه . وكذلك العالم بما في دخول النار من الألم إذا علم أنه لا نفع له في دخولها البتة ، وعلم أنه لو دخلها حصلت آلام عظيمة في جسده ، فإنه مع هذا العلم لا يدخلها البتة . فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن أفعالنا يجب وقوعها بحسب دواعينا ، ويجب انتفاؤها بحسب صوارفنا . وذلك هو تمام المقدمة الأولى . وأما المقدمة الثانية : فهي قولنا : إن الأمر لما كان كذلك ، وجب أن يكون حدوث أفعالنا بنا . والدليل عليه : أنه لو كان المحدث لها غيرنا ، لصح
Chapter Two: Narrative of the proofs by which they held fast in establishing that the servant is an originator (mūjid). Know that they argued with many aspects: The first proof: They said: Our actions must occur in accordance with our motives (dawā'īnā), and must be negated in accordance with our aversions (karāhātina). And that indicates their occurrence by us. As for the first premise: Its explanation is that the fasting person in the hot summer when his desire for drinking water intensifies, and the lawgiver (al-shāri') indicates that to him, and the physician indicates that to him, and he knows for certain that there is no consequence upon him in drinking that water, either immediately or in the future, then inevitably he will drink it. Likewise, the one who knows the pain of entering fire, when he knows that there is no benefit for him in entering it at all, and knows that if he entered it severe pains would occur in his body, then with this knowledge he will definitely not enter it. So it is established by what we have mentioned: that our actions must occur according to our motives, and must be negated according to our deterrents (sawārifinā). That is the completion of the first premise. As for the second premise: It is our statement: Since the matter is thus, it is necessary that the occurrence of our actions be by us. And the evidence for it is: If the one who brings them into existence (al-muḥdith) were other than us, then it would be valid...
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage introduces a new chapter (الفصل الثاني) which aims to present proofs (الدلائل) used by the Mu'tazilites (or proponents of free will) to establish that the servant (العبد) is the originator (موجد) of his actions. The author states that they argued with many aspects, and begins with the first proof (الحجة الأولى). The proof consists of two premises. The first premise: Our actions must occur in accordance with our motives (دواع) and must be negated in accordance with our dislikes (كراهات). The explanation: a fasting person in summer with intense thirst, when the religious law (الشارع) and the physician both permit drinking, and he knows there is no adverse consequence, will inevitably drink. Similarly, a person who knows the pain of fire and that there is no benefit in entering it will inevitably avoid it. Thus, actions necessarily follow motives and are repelled by deterrents. The second premise: Since this is the case, the occurrence of actions must be by us (بنا). The evidence: If the one who brings them into existence were other than us, then... (the text cuts off at the bottom of the page). The proof is incomplete here, but the intention is to argue that the necessary connection between our motives and actions shows that we are the true agents, because if an external agent were causing the action, the connection to motives would be unnecessary or coincidental.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The necessary connection between motives and actions implies determinism (i.e., actions are forced by external factors).
Correction: The author uses this connection to prove human agency: because actions align with our internal motives, they originate from us, not from an external force.

Checkpoint
What is the first premise of the first proof?
A) Human actions are random and not connected to motives.
B) Human actions must occur according to motives and be negated according to aversions.
C) Human actions are caused by God alone.
D) Human actions are impossible.

Key Takeaway
The first premise shows that human actions are causally connected to motives and aversions, which is essential for proving that the actions originate from the human agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The lesson says that when you truly know the pain of fire and see no benefit, you will never enter it. Examine your life: what do you truly know about the consequences of disobedience? If your actions do not match that knowledge, what does that reveal about the depth of your certainty?
?
Your daily routine—your prayers, your work, your speech—what does it reveal about your strongest motives? Are you willing to face the truth that your actions are a direct mirror of what you truly love and fear?
